There he saw a restrained and concentrated saint sitting below a tree, who looked delicate and accustomed to comfort.  {#20:4}

When the king saw his figure, his astonishment at that ascetic's figure was very great and unequalled.  {#20:5}

'O his colour, O his figure, O the loveliness of the noble man, O his tranquillity, O his perfection, O his disregard for pleasures!' [pg 101] =7= Adoring his feet and keeping him on his right side (he sat down), neither too far off nor too close by, and asked him with his hands clasped:  {#20:6}

'Though a young nobleman, you have entered the order; in an age fit for pleasure you exert yourself as a Śramaṇa, O ascetic; I want to hear you explain this.'  {#20:8}

"I am without a protector, O great king; there is nobody to protect me, I know no friend nor any one to have sympathy with me."  {#20:9}

Then king Śrēṇika, the ruler of Magadha, laughed: 'How should there be nobody to protect one so accomplished as you?'  {#20:10}

'I am the protector of religious men [^265]; O monk; enjoy pleasures together with your friends and relations; for it is a rare chance to be born as a human being.'  {#20:11}

"You yourself are without a protector, Śrēṇika, ruler of Magadha; and as you are without a protector, how can you protect anybody else?"  {#20:12}

When the saint had addressed this unprecedented speech to the king, who was greatly moved and astonished, and struck with astonishment, (he answered) [^266]:  {#20:13}

'I have horses, elephants, and subjects, a town and a seraglio, power and command: enjoy human pleasures.  {#20:14}

'In possession of so great means, which permit the owner to enjoy all pleasures, how could he be [pg 102] without protection? Reverend sir, you speak untruth.'  {#20:15}

"O king, you do not know the meaning and origin [^267] of (the word) 'without protection,' nor how one comes to be without protection or with protection, O ruler of men.  {#20:16}

"Hear, O great king, with an undistracted mind in what way a man can be said to be 'without protection,' and with what purpose I have said all this.  {#20:17}

"There is a town Kauśāmbī by name, which is among towns what Indra [^268] is (among the gods); there lived my father, who possessed great wealth.  {#20:18}

"In my childhood, O great king, I caught a very bad eye-disease and a severe burning fever in all my limbs, O ruler of men.  {#20:19}

"My eyes ached as if a cruel enemy thrust a sharp tool in the hollow of my body.  {#20:20}

"In the back, the heart [^269], and the head, I suffered [pg 103] dreadful and very keen pains equal to a stroke of lightning.  {#20:21}

"Then the best physicians came to my help, who cure by their medical art and by spells, who were versed in their science, and well knew spells and roots.  {#20:22}

"They tried to cure me according to the fourfold science [^270] which they had been taught; but they could not rid me of my pains: hence I say that I am without protection.  {#20:23}

"My father would have spent all he possessed, for my sake; but he could not rid me of my pains, hence I say that I am without protection.  {#20:24}

"My mother, O great king, was agonized with grief about her son; but she could not, &c.  {#20:25}

"O great king, my own brothers, the elder and younger ones, could not rid me of my pains, &c.  {#20:26}

"O great king, my own sisters, the elder and younger ones, could not, &c.  {#20:27}

"O great king, my loving and faithful wife moistened my breast with the tears of her eyes.  {#20:28}

"The poor lady did not eat, nor drink, nor bathe, nor use perfumes, wreaths, and anointment, with my knowledge or without it.  {#20:29}

"O great king, she did not leave [^271] my side even for a moment; but she could not rid me of my pains, hence I say that I am without protection.  {#20:30}

"Then I said: It is very hard to bear pains again and again in the endless Circle of Births.  {#20:31}

[pg 104]

"If I, for once, shall get rid of these great pains, I shall become a houseless monk, calm, restrained, and ceasing to act.  {#20:32}

"While I thought so, I fell asleep, O ruler of men; and after that night my pains had vanished.  {#20:33}

"Then in the morning of the next day I took leave of my relations and became a houseless monk, calm, restrained, and ceasing to act.  {#20:34}

"Thus I became the protector of myself and of others besides, of all living beings, whether they move or not.  {#20:35}

"My own Self is the river Vaitaraṇī, my own Self the Śālmalī tree [^272]; my own Self is the miraculous cow Kāmaduh, my own Self the park Nandana.  {#20:36}

"My own Self is the doer and undoer of misery and happiness; my own Self, friend and foe, according as I act well or badly.  {#20:37}

"But there is still another want of protection, O king; hear, therefore, O king, attentively with concentrated thoughts, how some easily discouraged men go astray after having adopted the Law of the Nirgranthas [^273].  {#20:38}

"If an ordained monk, through carelessness, does not strictly keep the great vows, if he does not restrain himself, but desires pleasure, then his fetters will not be completely cut off.  {#20:39}

"One who does not pay constant attention to his walking, his speaking, his begging, his receiving and keeping (of things necessary for a monk), and his [pg 105] easing nature [^274], does not follow the road trod by the Lord.  {#20:40}

"One who for a long time wears a shaven crown and mortifies himself, but who is careless with regard to the vows, and neglects penance and self-control, will not be a winner in the battle (of life).  {#20:41}

"He is empty like a clenched [^275] fist, (of no value) like an uncoined [^276] false Kārshāpaṇa or like a piece of glass resembling turquoise, he is held lightly by men of discernment.  {#20:42}

"He who has the character of a sinner, though he lays great stress on the outward signs of his calling [^277] as a means of living; he who does not control himself, though he pretends to do so; will come to grief for a long time.  {#20:43}

"As the poison Kālakūṭa kills him who drinks it; as a weapon cuts him who awkwardly handles it; as a Vētāla kills him who does not lay him; so the Law harms him who mixes it up with sensuality.  {#20:44}

"He who practises divination from bodily marks and dreams, who is well versed in augury and superstitious rites, who gains a sinful living by practising magic tricks [^278], will have no refuge at the time (of retribution).  {#20:45}

"The sinner, always wretched, goes from darkness [pg 106] to darkness, to utter misery; the unholy man who breaks the rules of monks, rushes, as it were, to hell, and to be born again as a brute.  {#20:46}

"He who accepts forbidden alms, viz. such food as he himself asks for, as has been bought for his sake, or as he gets regularly (as by right and custom), who like fire devours everything, will go to hell from here, after having sinned.  {#20:47}

"A cut-throat enemy will not do him such harm as his own perversity will do him; the man without pity will feel repentance in the hour of death.  {#20:48}

"In vain he adopts nakedness, who errs about matters of paramount interest; neither this world nor the next will be his; he is a loser in both respects in the world.  {#20:49}

"Thus the self-willed sinner who leaves the road of the highest Jinas, who with the appetite of an osprey is desirous of pleasure, will grieve in useless sorrow.  {#20:50}

"A wise man who hears this discourse, an instruction full of precious wisdom, and who deserts every path of the wicked, should walk the road of the great Nirgranthas.  {#20:51}

"He who possesses virtuous conduct and life, who has practised the best self-control, who keeps from sinful influences [^279], and who has destroyed his Karman, will reach (in the end) the greatest, best, and permanent place (viz. mukti),"  {#20:52}

Thus the austere and calm, great ascetic and great sage who kept great vows and possessed great fame, preached at great length this great sermon: the great duty of the Nirgranthas.  {#20:53}

[pg 107]

And king Śrēṇika, pleased, spoke thus: 'You have truly shown what it is to be without protection.  {#20:54}

'You have made the best use of human birth, you have made a true gain, O great sage, you are a protector (of mankind at large) and of your relations, for you have entered the path of the best Jinas.  {#20:55}

'You are the protector of all unprotected beings, O ascetic; I ask you to forgive me: I desire you to put me right.  {#20:56}

'That by asking you I have disturbed your meditation, and that I invited you to enjoy pleasures, all this you must forgive me.'  {#20:57}

When the lion of kings had thus, with the greatest devotion, praised the lion of houseless monks, he, together with his wives, servants, and relations, became a staunch believer in the Law, with a pure mind.  {#20:58}

The ruler of men, with the hair on his body joyfully erected, bowed his head (to the monk), keeping him on his right side, and departed.  {#20:59}

And the other, rich in virtues, protected by the three Guptis, and abstaining from injuring (living beings) in the three ways (viz. by thought, words, and acts), travelled about on the earth, free like a bird, and exempt from delusion.  {#20:60}

Thus I say.

[^261]: Atthadhammagaiṃ = arthadharmagati. I think this equal to artha dharma mōksha, though the commentators offer a different explanation by making gati mean jñāna. The phrase is derived from the typical expression kāmārthadharmamōksha by leaving out kāma, which of course could not be admitted by ascetics.

[^262]: He is identical with Bimbisāra of the Buddhists; see my edition of the Kalpa Sūtra, introduction, p. 2.

[^263]: The following verses prove that caitya denotes park here as the word is explained by the scholiast in IX, 9.

[^264]: Nandana is Indra's park.

[^265]: Bhadantāṇaṃ.

[^266]: The verb is wanting in this verse, and there is an apparent tautology in the words as they now stand. This is an obvious mark of a corruption in the text, which, however, I do not know how to remove by a plausible conjecture.

[^267]: Potthaṃ or pocchaṃ. The commentators are at a loss to give an etymology of this word, or rather have a choice of them to offer, which comes to the same thing, and proves that nothing certain was known. If potthā is the correct form, it may be derived from pra + ut + sthā, and mean 'origin;' if pocchā or pucca is the right spelling it is pṛcchā, and may mean 'etymology.'

[^268]: Purāṇa purabhēdaṇī. As usual the commentators give a purely etymological explanation. But it is obvious that purabhēdana must have a similar meaning as purandara = Indra, or purabhid Śiva. The latter word occurs in later literature only, and, besides, Siva does not yet seem to have been generally acknowledged as the supreme god, when and where the Jaina Sūtras were composed. The Vedic word pūrbhid, 'destroyer of castles,' also presents itself as an analogy; though it is not yet the exclusive epithet of a god, it is frequently applied to Indra.

[^269]: To render antariccha or antarittha. The Guzeratī translation renders it hṛday a.

[^270]: Cāuppāya = Catuḥpāda. Four branches of medical science are intended.

[^271]: Phiṭṭai = bhraśyati, Hēmacandra's Prākṛt Grammar, iv, 177.

[^272]: See above, p. 94.

[^273]: The verses 38-53 are apparently a later addition because (1) the subject treated in them is not connected with that of the foregoing part, and (2) they are composed in a different metre.

[^274]: These are the five Samitis, see above, p. 50.

[^275]: Pollā or pullā, explained antaḥ-sushira 'hollow in the middle.'

[^276]: Ayantita = ayantrita. My translation is but conjectural. Perhaps the regular coins are not meant, but stamped lumps of metal, which were current long before coins were introduced.

[^277]: Literally, 'the flag of the seers;' the broom &c. are meant.

[^278]: Kuhēḍavigjā.

[^279]: Nirāsava = nirāsrava. For the āsravas, see p. 55, note [^165].

* * *

[pg 108]

## TWENTY-FIRST LECTURE. SAMUDRAPĀLA.

In Campā there lived a Śrāvaka, the merchant Pālita, who was a disciple of the noble and venerable Mahāvīra.  {#21:1}

As a Śrāvaka he was well versed in the doctrines of the Nirgranthas. Once he went by boat to the town of Pihuṇḍa on business.  {#21:2}

A merchant gave him his daughter while he was doing business in Pihuṇḍa. When she was big with child, he took her with him on his returning home.  {#21:3}

Now the wife of Pālita was delivered of a child at sea; as the boy was born at sea (samudra), he was named Samudrapāla.  {#21:4}

Our merchant, the Śrāvaka, went leisurely to Kampā, to his house; in his house the boy grew up surrounded by comfort.  {#21:5}

He studied the seventy-two arts, and acquired knowledge of the world [^280]; he was in the bloom of {#21:7}

youth, and had a fine figure and good looks. (6) His father procured him a beautiful wife, Rūpiṇī, with whom he amused himself in his pleasant palace, like a Dōgundaga god [^281]. 

Once upon a time he saw from the window of his palace a man sentenced to death, dressed for execution, on his way to the place of execution.  {#21:8}

[pg 109]

Agitated by what he saw, Samudrapāla spoke thus: 'Of wicked actions this is the bad result.'  {#21:9}

He became enlightened at once, the venerable man, and he was immensely agitated; he took leave of his parents, and entered the state of houselessness.  {#21:10}

Abandoning the great distress to which the worldly [^282] are liable, the great delusion, and whatever causes fear, one should adopt the Law of monks [^283], the vows, the virtues, and the (endurance of) calamities.  {#21:11}

One should keep the five great vows, viz. not to kill, to speak the truth, not to steal, to be chaste, to have no property whatever; a wise man should follow the Law taught by the Jinas.  {#21:12}

A monk should have compassion on all beings, should be of a forbearing character, should be restrained and chaste, and abstaining from everything sinful; he should live with his senses under control.  {#21:13}

Now and then [^284] he should travel in one country, [pg 110] taking into consideration its resources and his own ability; like a lion he should not be frightened by any noise; and whatever words he hears, he should not make an improper reply.  {#21:14}

In utter indifference he should walk about, and bear everything, be it pleasant or unpleasant; he should not approve of everything everywhere, nor care for [^285] respectful treatment or blame.  {#21:15}

There are many opinions here among men, which a monk places in their true light; there will rise many dangerous and dreadful calamities, caused by gods, men, or animals, which are difficult to be borne and cause easily-discouraged men to sink under them; but a monk who comes in contact with them will not be afraid, like a stately elephant at the head of the battle.  {#21:16}

Cold and heat, flies and gnats, unpleasant feelings, and many diseases attack the body; without flinching [^286] he should bear them, and should [pg 111] not recall to his memory the pleasures he once enjoyed.  {#21:18}

Giving up love, hatred, and delusion, a monk who is always careful and who is steadfast even as Mount Mēru cannot be shaken by the storm, should bear calamities, guarding himself.  {#21:19}

A great sage should be neither too elevated by pride nor too humble, he should not care for respectful treatment nor blame; an ascetic who has ceased (to act), will by means of his simplicity enter the path of Nirvāṇa.  {#21:20}

He is neither grieved nor pleased (by anything) [^287], he abandons his relations with men, he ceases (to act), is intent on the benefit of his soul, he strives for the highest good (viz. mukti), and uses the means to reach it, free from sorrow, egoism, and any kind of property.  {#21:21}

A merciful (monk) should use beds distant from others, which are not got ready for his sake [^288] nor strewn (with leaves or things considered to be possessed of life); he should sustain such hardships as the sages are accustomed to.  {#21:22}

The great sage (Samudrapāla), understanding the sacred lore and practising completely the best Law, shone forth like the sun in the sky, being possessed of the highest knowledge and glory.  {#21:23}

Having annihilated his Karman both meritorious [pg 112] and sinful, being steadfast [^289], and free from all fetters, Samudrapāla crossed the ocean-like Flood of worldly existence and obtained exemption from transmigration.  {#21:24}

Thus I say.

[^280]: To render nītikōvida.

[^281]: For Dōgundaga, see above, p. 88, note [^235].

[^282]: Saggantha = sagrantha, which is obviously the opposite of nirgrantha. The commentators correct saṃgaṃtha in saṃgaṃ ca. The original reading is in MS. B. A. has saṃgaṃtha, and so had C. originally, but it corrects the tha into ca. According to the commentators we should translate: abandoning worldly attachment which causes great distress, great delusion, black (Lēśyā), and dangers, one should, &c.

[^283]: Paryāya-dharma. Paryāya means a state under which a substance presents itself. Here is meant the state of the soul in pravrajyā, i.e. śrāmaṇya-paryāya; compare the expressions chadmastha-paryāya and kēvali-paryāya. Paryāya-dharma is here equal to prayrajyā-dharma, Law of the monks.

[^284]: Kālēṇa kālaṃ, the commentators supply kurvan, and explain the passage as follows: kālēna, i.e. in a paurushī (four [pg 110] hours) less one quarter of it, kālam, i.e. what is proper for the time. The meaning would be 'doing at every time what is proper or prescribed to do at it.' But this explanation looks very artificial; I think that the expression kalēṇa kālaṃ is an adverb of the same type as majjhaṃ majjhēṇa and many others.

[^285]: Saṃjae. This word may be saṃyata in this place; but in verse 20, where the same line occurs again, it cannot be so interpreted, because there the word saṃjae occurs twice; once it has the meaning of saṃjata, but in the passage under discussion it must be a verb, and it is rendered there sañjayet = saṅgaṃ kuryāt by the commentators.

[^286]: Akukkuō, translated akukūja, derived from the root kūj 'to warble, to groan;' it would therefore mean 'without complaint.' But in I, 30 we have appakukkuē, derived from the root kuc to bend, to be crooked,' and it is rendered alpaspandana. The same meaning applies in the present case.

[^287]: This is the meaning commonly given to the frequently occurring phrase arairaisahe. Another interpretation is: saṃyamā-saṃyamavishayē, tābhyāṃ na bādhatē.

[^288]: Nirōvalēvāi = nirupalipta. By upalēpa may be meant 'dirt,' but the author of the Avacūri explains upalēpa as consisting in abhishvaṅga affection: It is almost impossible to render satisfactorily so vague an expression.

[^289]: Nirangaṇa = saṃyamē niścala, immovable with regard to self-control.

* * *

## TWENTY-SECOND LECTURE. RATHANĒMI.

In the town of Śauryapura [^290] there was a powerful king, Vasudēva by name, who possessed the characteristic marks of a king.  {#22:1}

He had two wives, Rōhiṇī and Dēvakī; each of them had a beloved son, Rāma and Kēśava.  {#22:2}

In the town of Śauryapura there was (another) powerful king, Samudravijaya by name, who possessed the characteristic marks of a king.  {#22:3}

His wife was Śivā by name; and her famous son was the venerable Arishṭanēmi, the saviour of the world and the lord of ascetics.  {#22:4}

This Arishṭanēmi, who was gifted with an excellent voice and possessed the thousand and eight lucky marks of the body, was a Gautama, and his skin was black.  {#22:5}

His body was strong like that of a bull, and hard [pg 113] like steel; he was well proportioned, and had a belly like that of a fish. {#22:6}

Kēśava asked the girl Rājīmatī [^291] in marriage for him. 

Now this daughter of an excellent king [^292] was virtuous and well looking; she possessed all lucky marks of the body, and shone forth like the lightning Saudāmanī.  {#22:7}

Her father said to the powerful Vāsudēva: 'Let the prince come here that I may give him my daughter.'  {#22:8}

He had taken a bath containing all (lucky) herbs, and had performed the customary ceremonies; he wore a suit of heavenly clothes and was decked out with ornaments.  {#22:9}

Riding on the best mast elephant [^293] of Vāsudēva he looked beautiful, like a jewel worn on the head.  {#22:10}

He sat under a raised umbrella, fanned by two chowries, and he was surrounded on all sides by a host of Daśārhas [^294] and by a complete army drawn [pg 114] up in rank and file, while the heavenly sound of musical instruments reached the sky.  {#22:11}

With such pomp and splendour the hero of the Vṛshṇis started from his own palace.  {#22:13}

On his way he saw animals, kept in cages and enclosures, overcome by fear and looking miserable.  {#22:14}

Seeing them on the point of being killed for the sake of their flesh, and to be eaten afterwards, the great sage spoke to his charioteer [^295] thus:  {#22:15}

'Why are [^296] all these animals, which desire to be happy, kept in cages and enclosures?'  {#22:16}

Then the charioteer answered: 'Lucky are these animals because at thy wedding they will furnish food for many people.'  {#22:17}

Having heard these words, which announced the slaughter of many animals, the great sage, full of compassion and kindness to living beings, meditated thus:  {#22:18}

'If for my sake many living beings are killed, I shall not obtain happiness in the next world.'  {#22:19}

Then the famous man presented the charioteer with his pair of earrings, his neck-chain, and all his ornaments.  {#22:20}

When he had formed his resolution, the gods [pg 115] descended (from heaven), according to the established custom, to celebrate, with great pomp together with their retinue, the event of his renunciation.  {#22:21}

Surrounded by gods and men, and sitting on an excellent palankin, the Venerable One left Dvārakā and ascended mount Raivataka [^297]  {#22:22}

On arriving at the park he descended from his excellent palankin, surrounded by a crowd of thousands, and then his renunciation took place, while the moon was in conjunction with Citrā [^298].  {#22:23}

Then he himself plucked out his delightfully-perfumed, soft, and curled hair in five handfuls.  {#22:24}

And Vāsudēva said to that subduer of the senses, who had plucked out his hair: 'O lord of ascetics, may you soon obtain what you wish and desire.  {#22:25}

'Increase in knowledge, faith, and right conduct, in forbearance and perfection!'  {#22:26}

In this manner Rāma and Kēsava, the Daśārhas, and many people paid homage to Arishṭanēmi and then returned to the town of Dvārakā.  {#22:27}

When the daughter of the king heard of the ordination of the Jina, laughter and gaiety forsook her, and she was overwhelmed with affliction' [^299].  {#22:28}

[pg 116]

Rājīmatī thought: 'Shame upon my life, that I have been forsaken by him! it is better I should turn nun.'  {#22:29}

Firm and decided she cut off her tresses which were black like bees and dressed with a brush and comb [^300].  {#22:30}

And Vāsudēva said to her who had cut off her hair, and subdued her senses: 'Lady, cross the dreadful ocean of the Saṃsāra without difficulty!'  {#22:31}

When she had entered the order, the virtuous and very learned lady induced there many people, her relations and servants, to enter the order too.  {#22:32}

On her way to mount Raivataka it began to rain; her clothes being wet, she entered a cave and waited there in the darkness while it was raining.  {#22:33}

She took off her clothes and was naked as she was born, thus she was seen by Rathanēmi [^301], whose [pg 117] (peace of) mind became (thereby) disturbed; and afterwards she saw him.  {#22:34}

She was frightened when she discovered herself alone with the monk; folding her arms over her breast she sank down trembling.  {#22:35}

When the prince, Samudravijaya's son, saw her frightened and trembling, he spoke the following words:  {#22:36}

'I am Rathanēmi, O dear, beautiful, sweetly-speaking lady! Do accept me for your lover, O slender one [^302], you shall have no cause to complain.  {#22:37}

'Come, let us enjoy pleasures, for it is a rare chance to be born a human being; after we have enjoyed pleasures, we shall enter on the path of the Jinas.'  {#22:38}

When Rājīmatī perceived that Rathanēmi's strength of will was broken, and temptation had got the better of him, she did not lose her presence of mind and defended her Self on that occasion.  {#22:39}

The daughter of the best king, true to self-control and her vows, maintained the honour of her clan and family, and her virtue, and spoke to him:  {#22:40}

'If you owned the beauty of Vaiśramaṇa [^303], the pleasing manners of Nalakūbara [^304], if you were like Purandara [^305] himself, I should have no desire for you.  {#22:41}

[pg 118]

'Fie upon you, famous knight, who want to quaff the vomited drink for the sake of this life; it would be better for you to die [^306].  {#22:42}

'I am the daughter of the Bhōga-king [^307], and you are an Andhakavṛshṇi; being born in a noble family let us not become like Gandhana-snakes [^308]; firmly practise self-control!  {#22:43}

'If you fall in love with every woman you see, you will be without hold like the Haṭha-plant [^309], driven before the wind.  {#22:44}

'As a herdsman or a keeper of goods does not own the things (he has the care of), so you will not truly own Śramaṇahood.'  {#22:45}

Having heard these well-spoken words of the virtuous lady, he returned to the Law like an elephant driven by the hook [^310].  {#22:46}

Protected in thoughts, words, and acts, subduing his senses and keeping the vows, he practised true Śramaṇahood throughout life.  {#22:47}

[pg 119]

After practising severe austerities both of them became Kēvalins, and having completely annihilated their Karman, they reached the highest perfection.  {#22:48}

Thus act the enlightened, the wise, the clever ones; they turn from pleasures as did this best of men [^311].  {#22:49}

Thus I say.

[^290]: According to the Brahmanical account Vasudēva lived in Mathurā. The name given to the town by the Jainas is apparently derived from Śauri, an epithet of Kṛshṇa, whose grandfather was Śūra. Soriyapura may be Śaurikapura or Śauryapura. The latter rendering adopted by our commentators is based on a wrong etymology.

[^291]: Rāīmai, Rāimaī, and Rāyamatī are the forms of her name in Prākṛt; the spellings Rājimatī and Rājamatī are also met with in Sanskrit.

[^292]: Viz. Ugrasēna. He was placed on the throne by Kṛshṇa on the death of Kaṃsa, cf. Vishṇu Purāṇa V, 21. He and Dēvaka were the sons of Āhuka, Kaṃsa was a son of Ugrasēna, and Dēvakī a daughter of Dēvaka, loc. cit. IV, 14. According to the legend of Kṛshṇa, as told by the Brahmans and Jainas, Jarāsandha afterwards repeatedly attacked Mathurā. Kṛshṇa therefore built Dvārakā on the shore of the western ocean, and sent thither the Yādava tribe, loc. cit. V, 22 and 23. The events narrated in the text must be understood to have occurred in Dvārakā, as is evident from verse 21.

[^293]: Gandhahastin, an elephant of the best class, whose very smell is sufficient, as is believed, to frighten common elephants, see verse 55.

[^294]: Dasāra in Prākṛt. They are a clan descended from Yadu.

[^295]: In verse 10 Arishṭanēmi rides on an elephant, but in the sequel he is supposed to travel in a car. Unless the poet can be charged with having made this blunder, which I think just possible, verse 10 must be considered a later addition.

[^296]: The form of the verb acchahiṃ for acchanti is worthy of note, because hiṃ as ending of the third person plural belongs to Apabhraṃśa. It is interesting to find a true Apabhraṃśa form in a text so old as ours, for it seems to prove that at all times Apabhraṃśa went along with the common Prākṛt, a vulgar or low with a high middle-Indian language.

[^297]: Raivataka is mount Girnār in Kaṭhiawāḍ. The hill is one of the most sacred places of the Jainas, and is covered with temples of the Jinas. It is also sacred to the Hindus on account of its connection with the history of Kṛshṇa. The poetical description of mount Raivataka forms the subject of the fourth sarga of the Śiśupālavadha by Māgha.

[^298]: The lunar mansion, the chief star of which is Spica or α Virginis.

[^299]: The lamentation of Rājīmatī on her husband's becoming an ascetic forms the subject of a curious Sanskrit poem called Nēmidūtakāvya, by Vikrama, son of Saṅghaṇa, which has been edited in [pg 116] the Kāvyamālā of 1886. It is what is technically called a samasyāpūraṇa or gloss. The last line of each stanza is taken from the Mēghadūta of Kālidāsa, and the first three lines are added by the poet to make the whole fit the circumstances of his tale.

[^300]: Kuccaphaṇaga, in Sanskrit kūrcaphanaka. According to the scholiasts phanaka is a comb made of bamboo.--I have translated, 'cut off her tresses,' but literally it is: 'plucked out her hair.' However, I do not think that women also are to pluck out their hair.

[^301]: Rathanēmi was her husband's elder brother. According to a legend told in Haribhadra's Ṭīkā of the Daśavaikālika Sūtra (see Leumann in the Journal of the German Oriental Society, vol. 46, p. 597), Rathanēmi fell in love with Rājīmatī. But that lady in order to make him see his wrong, vomited a sweet beverage she had drunk, in a cup and offered it him. On his turning away with disgust she explained to him her meaning: she too had been vomited, as it were, by Arishṭanēmi, notwithstanding which he wanted to have her. She then taught him the Jaina creed, and he became a monk.

[^302]: Suyaṇu = sutanu. This may, however, be a proper name, a synonym of Rājīmatī, for according to the Harivaṃśa 2029 and the Vishṇu Purāṇa IV, 14, Sutanu was a daughter of Ugrasēna.

[^303]: Vaiśramaṇa is a Prākṛt spelling for Vaiśravaṇa = Kubēra.

[^304]: Nalakūbara is Vaiśramaṇa's son.

[^305]: Indra.

[^306]: The verses 42, 43, 44, 46 have been received in the Daśavaikālika Sūtra II, 7-10, see Leumann's edition of that Sūtra quoted in the note, p. 116. A metrical German translation will be found in the same place.

[^307]: On the Bhōgas see p. 71, note 2. It is perhaps here misspelt for Bhōja. In the Vishṇu Purāṇa, Kaṃsa, Ugrasēna's son, is twice called Bhōjarāja (see Wilson's translation, ed. Hall, vol. iv, pp. 260, 271), in contradiction to the common tradition which makes him an Andhaka, compare Patañjali on Pāṇini IV, 1, 114.

[^308]: There are said to be two kinds of snakes, the gandhana and the agandhana. The former can be made to suck the poison from the wound they have inflicted; the other will rather die than do so. Cf. Leumann, loc. cit., p. 597, note *.

[^309]: Pistia Stratiotes, an aquatic plant.

[^310]: Dēvēndra here refers to the story of the Nūpurapaṇḍita, of which he gives a small portion in Prākṛt. The whole story 's related in the Pariśishṭaparvan of Hēmacandra, see the introduction to my edition of that work in the Bibliotheca Indica.

[^311]: Compare the last verse of the Ninth Lecture.

* * *

## TWENTY-THIRD LECTURE [^312]. KĒŚI AND GAUTAMA.

There was a Jina, Pārśva [^313] by name, an Arhat, worshipped by the people, who was thoroughly enlightened and omniscient, a prophet of the Law, and a Jina.  {#23:1}

And there was a famous disciple of this Light of [pg 120] the World, the young Śramaṇa Kēśi, who had completely mastered the sciences and right conduct.  {#23:2}

He possessed the light of Śruta and Avadhi knowledge [^314], and was surrounded by a crowd of disciples; wandering from village to village he arrived in the town of Śrāvastī.  {#23:3}

In the district of that town there is a park, called Tinduka; there he took up his abode in a pure place to live and sleep in.  {#23:4}

Now at that time there lived the Prophet of the Law, the Jina, who in the whole world is known as the venerable Vardhamāna.  {#23:5}

And there was a famous disciple of this Light of the World, the venerable Gautama by name, who had completely mastered the sciences and right conduct.  {#23:6}

He knew the twelve Aṅgas, was enlightened, and was surrounded by a crowd of disciples; wandering from village to village he too arrived in Śrāvastī.  {#23:7}

In the district of that town there is a park Kōshṭhaka; there he took up his abode in a pure place to live and sleep in.  {#23:8}

The young Śramaṇa Kēśi and the famous Gautama, both lived there, protecting themselves (by the Guptis) and being careful.  {#23:9}

The pupils of both, who, controlled themselves, who practised austerities, who possessed virtues, and who protected their Self, made the following reflection:  {#23:10}

[pg 121]

'Is our Law the right one, or is the other Law [^315] the right one? are our conduct and doctrines right, or the other?  {#23:11}

'The Law as taught by the great sage Pārśva, which recognises but four vows [^316], or the Law taught by Vardhamāna, which enjoins five vows?  {#23:12}

'The Law which forbids clothes (for a monk), hr that which (allows) an under and upper garment? Both pursuing the same end, what has caused their difference?'  {#23:13}

Knowing the thoughts of their pupils, both Kēśi and Gautama made up their minds to meet each other.  {#23:14}

Gautama, knowing what is proper and what is due to the older section (of the church), went to the Tinduka park, accompanied by the crowd, his pupils.  {#23:15}

When Kēśi, the young monk, saw Gautama approach, he received him with all becoming attention.  {#23:16}

He at once offered Gautama the four pure kinds of straw and hay [^317] to sit upon.  {#23:17}

Kēśi, the young Śramaṇa, and the famous Gautama, sitting together, shone forth with a lustre like that of sun and moon.  {#23:18}

There assembled many heretics out of curiosity, and many thousands of laymen;  {#23:19}

Gods, Dānavas, Gandharvas, Yakshas, Rākshasas, [pg 122] and Kinnaras (assembled there), and there came together invisible ghosts [^318] too.  {#23:20}

Kēśi said to Gautama, 'I want to ask you something, holy man.' Then to these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply: "Sir, ask whatever you like." Then with his permission Kēśi spoke to Gautama:  {#23:21}

'The Law taught by the great sage Pārśva, recognises but four vows, whilst that of Vardhamāna enjoins five.  {#23:23}

'Both Laws pursuing the same end, what has caused this difference? Have you no misgivings about this twofold Law, O wise man?'  {#23:24}

Then to these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply: "Wisdom recognises the truth of the Law and the ascertainment of true things.  {#23:25}

"The first [^319] saints were simple but slow of understanding, the last saints prevaricating and slow of understanding, those between the two simple and wise; hence there are two forms of the Law [^320].  {#23:26}

[pg 123]

"The first could but with difficulty understand the precepts of the Law, and the last could only with difficulty observe them, but those between them easily understood and observed them."  {#23:27}

'Well, Gautama, you possess wisdom, you have destroyed my doubt; but I have another doubt which you must explain to me, Gautama.  {#23:28}

'The Law taught by Vardhamāna forbids clothes, but that of the great sage Pārśva allows an under and upper garment.  {#23:29}

'Both Laws pursuing the same end, what has caused this difference? Have you no misgivings about this twofold Law, O wise man?'  {#23:30}

To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply: "Deciding the matter by their superior knowledge, (the Tīrthakaras) have fixed what is necessary for carrying out the Law.  {#23:31}

"The various outward marks (of religious men) have been introduced in order that people might recognise them as such; the reason for the characteristic marks is their usefulness for religious life and their distinguishing character.  {#23:32}

"Now the opinion (of the Tīrthakaras) is that knowledge, faith. and right conduct are the true causes of final liberation, (and not the outward marks)."  {#23:33}

'Well, Gautama, you possess wisdom, you have destroyed my doubt; but I have another doubt, which you must explain to me, Gautama.  {#23:34}

'Gautama, you stand in the midst of many [pg 124] thousand (foes) who make an attack on you; how have you vanquished them [^321]?  {#23:35}

"By vanquishing one, five are vanquished; by vanquishing five, ten are vanquished; by this tenfold victory, I vanquish all foes."  {#23:36}

Kēśi said to Gautama: 'Whom do you call a foe?' To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply:  {#23:37}

"Self is the one invincible foe, (together with the four) cardinal passions [^322], (viz. anger, pride, deceit, and greed, they are five) and the (five) senses (make ten). These (foes), O great sage, I have regularly vanquished."  {#23:38}

'Well, Gautama, &c. (as in verse 28).  {#23:19}

'We see many beings in this world who are bound by fetters; how have you got rid of your fetters and are set free, O sage?'  {#23:40}

"Having cut off all fetters, and having destroyed them by the right means, I have got rid of my fetters and am set free, O sage."  {#23:41}

Kēśi said to Gautama: 'What do you call fetters?' [pg 125] To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply:  {#23:42}

"Love, hatred, &c., are heavy fetters, attachment is a dangerous one; having regularly destroyed them, I live up to the rules of conduct."  {#23:43}

'Well, Gautama, &c. (as in verse 28).  {#23:44}

'O Gautama, in the innermost heart there grows a plant which brings forth poisonous fruit; how have you torn it out?'  {#23:45}

"I have thoroughly clipped that plant, and torn it out altogether with its roots; thus I have got rid of the poisonous fruit."  {#23:46}

Kēśi said to Gautama, 'What do you call that plant?' To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply:  {#23:47}

"Love of existence is that dreadful plant which brings forth dreadful fruit; having regularly torn it out, I live pleasantly."  {#23:48}

'Well, Gautama, &c. (as in verse 28).  {#23:49}

'Gautama, there is blazing up a frightful fire which burns [^323] the embodied beings; how have you put it out?'  {#23:50}

"Taking water, excellent water, from (the river) produced by the great cloud, I always pour it over my body; thus sprinkled the fire does not burn me."  {#23:51}

Kēśi said to Gautama, 'What do you call the fire?' To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply:  {#23:52}

"The passions are the fire; knowledge, a virtuous life, and penances are the water; sprinkled [pg 126] with the drops of knowledge the fire of the passions is extinguished and does not burn me."  {#23:53}

'Well, Gautama, &c. (as in verse 28).  {#23:54}

'The unruly, dreadful, bad horse, on which you sit, runs about, Gautama! how comes it to pass that it does not run off with you?'  {#23:55}

"I govern it well in its course by the bridle of knowledge; it does not go astray with me, it keeps to the right path."  {#23:56}

Kēśi said to Gautama, 'What do you call this horse?' To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply:  {#23:57}

"The mind is that unruly, dreadful, bad horse; I govern it by the discipline of the Law (so that it becomes a well-) trained Kambōja-steed [^324]."  {#23:58}

'Well, Gautama, &c. (as in verse 28).  {#23:59}

'There are many bad roads in this world, which lead men astray; how do you avoid, Gautama, going astray as you are on the road?'  {#23:60}

"They all are known to me, those who are in the right path and those who have chosen a wrong path; therefore I do not go astray, O sage!"  {#23:61}

Kēśi said to Gautama, 'What do you call the path?' To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply:  {#23:62}

"The heterodox and the heretics have all chosen a wrong path; the right path is that taught by the Jinas; it is the most excellent path."  {#23:63}

'Well, Gautama, &c. (as in verse 28).  {#23:64}

'Is there a shelter, a refuge, a firm ground for the beings carried away by the great flood of water? do you know the island, O Gautama?' [pg 127] =66= "There is a large, great island in the midst of water, which is not inundated by the great flood of water."  {#23:65}

Kēśi said to Gautama, 'What do you call this island?' To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply:  {#23:67}

"The flood is old age and death, which carry away living beings; Law is the island, the firm ground, the refuge, the most excellent shelter."  {#23:68}

'Well, Gautama, &c. (as in verse 28).  {#23:69}

'On the ocean with its many currents there drifts a boat; how will you, Gautama, on board of it reach the opposite shore?'  {#23:70}

"A boat that leaks will not reach the opposite shore; but a boat that does not leak, will reach it."  {#23:71}

Kēśi said to Gautama, 'What do you call this boat?' To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply:  {#23:72}

"The body is the boat, life is the sailor, and the Circle of Births is the ocean which is crossed by the great sages."  {#23:73}

'Well, Gautama, &c. (as in verse 28).  {#23:74}

'In this dreadfully dark gloom there live many beings; who will bring light into the whole world of living beings?'  {#23:75}

"The spotless sun has risen which illuminates the whole world; he will bring light into the whole world of living beings."  {#23:76}

Kēśi said to Gautama, 'What do you call this sun?' To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply:  {#23:77}

"Risen has he who put an end to the Circle of [pg 128] Births, the omniscient Jina, the luminary, who brings light into the whole world of living beings."  {#23:78}

'Well, Gautama, &c. (as in verse 28).  {#23:79}

'Do you, O sage, know a safe, happy, and quiet place for living beings which suffer from pains [^325] of body and mind?'  {#23:80}

"There is a safe place in view of all, but difficult of approach, where there is no old age nor death, no pain nor disease."  {#23:81}

Kēśi said to Gautama, 'What is  this place called?' To these words of Kēśi Gautama made the following reply:  {#23:82}

"It is what is called Nirvāṇa, or freedom from pain, or perfection, which is in view of all; it is the safe, happy, and quiet place which the great sages reach.  {#23:83}

"That is the eternal place, in view of all, but difficult of approach. Those sages who reach it are free from sorrows, they have put an end to the stream of existence."  {#23:84}

'Well, Gautama, you possess wisdom, you have destroyed my doubt; obeisance to you, who are not troubled by doubts, who are the ocean, as it were, of all Sūtras.'  {#23:85}

After his doubt had been solved, Kēśi, of enormous sanctity, bowed his head to the famous Gautama.  {#23:86}

And in the pleasant (Tinduka park) he sincerely adopted the Law of the five vows, which was proclaimed by the first Tīrthakara, according to the teaching of the last Tīrthakara.  {#23:87}

[pg 129]

In that meeting of Kēśi and Gautama, knowledge and virtuous conduct were for ever brought to eminence, and subjects of the greatest importance were settled.  {#23:88}

The whole assembly was greatly pleased and fixed their thoughts on the right way. They praised Kēśi and Gautama: 'May the venerable ones show us favour!'  {#23:89}

Thus I say.

[^312]: In this lecture we have a very interesting legend about the way in which the union of the old church of Pārśva and the new church of Mahāvīra was brought about. A revival of this ancient difference seems to have caused the united church afterwards to divide again into the present Śvētāmbara and Digambara sects. They do not continue the two primitive churches, but seem to have grown out of the united church.

[^313]: Pārśva is the last but one Tīrthakara, his Nirvāṇa took place 250 years before that of Mahāvīra. This statement, which has been generally accepted, is, however, in seeming contradiction to the account of our text, according to which a disciple of Pārśva, who is called a young monk kumāra-śramaṇa, met Gautama, i.e. Sudharman, the disciple of Mahāvīra. We therefore must take the word disciple, sīse, as paramparāśishya, that is not in its literal sense. See note [^320], .

[^314]: These are the second and third kinds of knowledge according to the Jaina classification. Śruta is the knowledge derived from the sacred books, and avadhi is limited or conditioned knowledge. See Bhandarkar, Report, p. 106.

[^315]: Viz. the Law of Pārśva or the Law of Mahāvīra.

[^316]: Hence it is called kāujjāma cāturyāma. Chastity (maithunaviramaṇa) was not explicitly enumerated, but it was understood to be contained in the fourth commandment: to have no property (aparigraha).

[^317]: The four kinds of straw are: sālī vīhī koddava rālaga, to which is added hay: raṇṇe taṇāṇi.

[^318]: Bhūya = bhūta, explained Vyantara. The vantara or vānamantara are a class of ghosts. The second part of the word apparently is tara 'crossing,' and the first seems to contain an accusative vaṃ or vāṇamaṃ which may be connected with viha or vyōman 'air.'

[^319]: Those under the first Tīrthakaras.

[^320]: The meaning of this explanation is as follows. As the vow of chastity is not explicitly mentioned among Pārśva's four vows, but was understood to be implicitly enjoined by them, it follows that only such men as were of an upright disposition and quick understanding would not go astray by observing the four vows literally, i.e. by not abstaining from sexual intercourse, as it was not expressly forbidden.--The argumentation in the text presupposes a decay of the morals of the monastic order to have occurred between Pārśva and Mahāvīra, and this is possible only on the assumption of a sufficient interval of time having [pg 123] elapsed between the last two Tīrthakaras. And this perfectly agrees with the common tradition that Mahāvīra came 250 years after Pārśva.

[^321]: This question does not refer to the difference in doctrines between Pārśva and Mahāvīra, but is discussed here, as the commentator states, for the benefit of the pupils of both sages who are engaged in conversation. I think, however, that this and the following questions are asked and answered here by the disciples of the two Tīrthakaras for a better reason than that given by the scholiast. For in them the leading topics of Jainism are treated in a symbolical way. Gautama at once understands the true meaning of the similes and interprets them to the satisfaction of Kēśi. In this way the unity in doctrine subsisting between the Law of Pārśva and that of Mahāvīra is demonstrated to the hearers of the dispute, after the differences had been explained away.

[^322]: Kashāya.

[^323]: In the original 'fire' is put in the plural because the four kashāyas or cardinal passions are denoted by it.

[^324]: Kanthaka, see above, p. 47, note 2.

[^325]: Māṇasē dukkhē stands for māṇasēhiṃ dukkhēhiṃ. It is an interesting instance of the dropping of case affixes, which probably was more frequent in the vernacular.

* * *

## TWENTY-FOURTH LECTURE. THE SAMITIS.

The eight articles [^326] of the creed are the Samitis and the Guptis; there are five Samitis and three Guptis.  {#24:1}

The Samitis [^327] are: 1. īryā-samiti (going by paths trodden by men, beasts, carts, &c., and looking carefully so as not to occasion the death of any living creature); 2. bhāshā-samiti (gentle, salutary, sweet, righteous speech); 3. ēshaṇā-samiti (receiving alms in a manner to avoid the forty-two faults that are laid down); 4. ādāna-samiti (receiving and keeping of the things necessary for [pg 130] religious exercises, after having carefully examined them); 5. uccāra-samiti (performing the operations of nature in an unfrequented place). The three Guptis (which are here included in the term Samiti in its wider application) are: 1. mano-gupti (preventing the mind from wandering in the forest of sensual pleasures by employing it in contemplation, study, &c.); 2. vāg-gupti (preventing the tongue from saying bad things by a vow of silence, &c.); 3. kāya-gupti (putting the body in an immovable posture as in the case of Kāyōtsarga).  {#24:2}

The eight Samitis are thus briefly enumerated, in which the whole creed taught by the Jinas and set forth in the twelve Aṅgas, is comprehended.  {#24:3}

1\. The walking of a well-disciplined monk should be pure in four respects: in respect to 1. the cause [^328]; 2\. the time; 3. the road; 4. the effort [^329].  {#24:4}

The cause is: knowledge, faith, and right conduct; the time is day-time; the road excludes bad ways.  {#24:5}

The effort is fourfold, viz. as regards: 1. substance, 2. place, 3. time, and 4. condition of mind. Hear me explain them.  {#24:6}

With regard to substance: the (walking monk) should look with his eyes; with regard to place: the space of a yuga (i.e. four hastas or cubits); with regard to time: as long as he walks; and with regard to condition of mind: carefully [^330].  {#24:7}

He walks carefully who pays attention only to [pg 131] his walk and his body (executing it), whilst he avoids attending to the objects of sense, but (minds) his study, the latter in all five ways [^331].  {#24:8}

2\. To give way to: anger, pride, deceit and greed, laughter, fear, loquacity and slander [^332]; these eight faults should a well-disciplined monk avoid; he should use blameless and concise speech at the proper time.  {#24:9}

3\. As regards begging [^333], a monk should avoid the faults in the search [^334], in the receiving [^335], and in the use [^336] of the three kinds of objects, viz. food, articles of use, and lodging.  {#24:11}

A zealous monk should avoid in the first (i.e. in the search for alms) the faults occasioned either by the giver (udgama) or by the receiver (utpādana); in the second (i.e. in the receiving of alms) the faults inherent in the receiving; and in the use of the articles received, the four faults [^337].  {#24:12}

[pg 132]

4\. If a monk takes up or lays down the two kinds of things belonging to his general and [pg 133] supplementary [^338] outfit, he should proceed in the following way.  {#24:13}

[pg 134]

A zealous monk should wipe the thing after having inspected it with his eyes, and then he should take it up or put it down, having the Samiti in both respects [^339].  {#24:14}

5\. Excrements, urine, saliva, mucus, uncleanliness of the body, offals of food, waste things, his own body (when he is about to die), and everything of this description (is to be disposed of in the way to be described).  {#24:15}

\[A place may be not frequented and not seen (by people), or not frequented but seen, or frequented and not seen, or frequented and seen. (16)\] [^340] {#24:17}

In a place neither frequented nor seen by other people, which offers no obstacles to self-control, which is even, not covered with grass or leaves [^341], and has [pg 135] been brought into its present condition [^342] not long ago, which is spacious, has an inanimate surface-layer [^343], not too near (the village, &c.), not perforated by holes, and is exempt from insects and seeds--in such a place he should leave his excrements, &c. 

The five Samitis are thus briefly enumerated, I shall now explain in due order the three Guptis [^344].  {#24:19}

1\. There is, 1. truth; 2. untruth; 3. a mixture of truth and untruth; 4. a mixture of what is not true, and what is not untrue. The Gupti of mind refers to all four [^345].  {#24:20}

A zealous monk should prevent his mind from desires for the misfortune of somebody else [^346], from thoughts on acts which cause misery to living beings [^347], and from thoughts on acts which cause their destruction [^348].  {#24:21}

2\. The Gupti of speech is also of four kinds (referring to the four divisions as in verse 20).  {#24:22}

A zealous monk should prevent his speech from (expressing) desires, &c. (as in verse 21).  {#24:23}

3\. In standing, sitting, lying down, jumping, going, and in the use of his organs, a zealous monk should prevent his body from intimating obnoxious desires, [pg 136] from doing acts which cause misery to living beings, or which cause their destruction.  {#24:24}

These are the five Samitis for the practice of the religious life, and the Guptis for the prevention of everything sinful.  {#24:26}

This is the essence of the creed, which a sage should thoroughly put into practice; such a wise man will soon get beyond the Circle of Births.  {#24:27}

Thus I say.

[^326]: The word I have rendered 'article' is māyā, the Sanskrit form of which may be mātā or mātrā. The word is derived from the root mā 'to find room in,' and denotes that which includes in itself other things, see verse 3. The word may also mean mātṛ 'mother,' as Weber understands it. But this is an obviously intentional double meaning. {#24:24}

[^327]: The definitions placed in parentheses in the text are taken from Bhandarkar's Report for 1883-1884, p. 98, note †, p. 100, note *.

[^328]: Ālambana, literally support; explained: supported by which the mind becomes pure.

[^329]: Jāyaṇā = yatna; it consists chiefly in compassion with living creatures (jīvadayā).

[^330]: Upayukta.

[^331]: The 'five ways' are vācanā, &c., as explained in the Twenty-ninth Lecture, §§ 39-23, below, p. 165 f. The commentators supply kuryāt 'he should carry on his study.'

[^332]: Vikahā = vikathā, which does not occur in common Sanskrit. Perhaps it stands for vikatthā 'boasting.'

[^333]: Ēshaṇā.

[^334]: Gavēshaṇā.

[^335]: Grahaṇaishaṇā.

[^336]: Paribhōgaishaṇā.

[^337]: There are altogether forty-six faults to be avoided. As they are frequently alluded to in the sacred texts, a systematical enumeration and description of them according to the Dīpikā will be useful.

    There are sixteen udgama-dōshas by which food, &c. becomes unfit for a Jaina monk:

    1\. Ādhākarmika, the fault inherent in food, &c., which a layman has prepared especially for religious mendicants of whatever sect.

    2\. Auddēśika, is food, &c., which a layman has prepared for a particular monk.

[pg 132]

3\. Pūtika, is food, &c., which is pure on the whole, but contains particles impure on account of the first fault.

4\. Unmiśra, is food, &c., of which a part only had been especially prepared for the monk in question.

5\. Sthāpanākarmika, is food, &c., which has been reserved for the monk.

6\. Prābhṛtika, is food, &c., which has been prepared for some festivity.

7\. Prāduḥkaraṇa, when the layman has to light a lamp in order to fetch the alms for the monk.

8\. Krīta, when he has to buy the things.

9\. Prāmitya, when he has to fetch a ladle (? uddhāraka) in order to draw out the food, &c.

10\. Parāvṛtti, when he replaces bad particles of the food by good ones, and vice versa.

11\. Adhyāhṛta, when he has to fetch the food, &c., from some distance.

12\. Udbhinna, when he has to open locks before he gets at the food, &c.

13\. Mālāhṛta, when he has to take the food, &c., from some raised or underground place.

14\. Ācchidya, when the food, &c., was taken by force from somebody.

15\. Anisṛshṭa, when a man gives from a store he possesses in common with other men, without asking their permission.

16\. Adhyavapūra, when the mendicant calls while the dinner is being cooked, and for his sake more food is put in the pot on the fire.

(Some of these faults are enumerated in the Aupapātika Sūtra, § 96, III.)

There are sixteen utpādana-dōshas; or such faults as are occasioned by the monk's using some means to make the layman give him alms:

1\. Dhātrīkarman, when the monk plays with the layman's children.

2\. Dūtakarman, when he gives him information about what his people are doing.

3\. Nimitta, when he speaks in praise of almsgiving.

4\. Ājīvikā, when he makes his birth and family known to him.

[pg 133]

5\. Vapanīka, when he expatiates upon his misery.

6\. Cikitsā, when he cures sick people.

7\. Krōdhapiṇḍa, when he extorts alms by threats.

8\. Mānapiṇḍa, when he tells the layman that he has laid a wager with other monks that he would get alms from him.

9\. Māyāpiṇḍa, when he employs tricks or buffoonery in order to procure alms.

10\. Lōbhapiṇḍa, when he goes begging from a desire of good fare.

11\. Saṃstava-piṇḍa, when he flatters the layman.

12\. Vidyāpiṇḍa, when he makes a show of his learning; or when he conjures a god from whom to get alms.

13\. Mantradōsha, when he obliges the layman in some way or other.

14\. Cūrṇayōga, when he makes himself invisible and then takes away the food, &c.

15\. Yōgapiṇḍa, when he teaches people spells, tricks, &c.

16\. Mūlakarman, when he teaches them how to obviate evils by roots, charms, &c.

There are ten faults of grahaṇaishaṇā:

1\. Saṅkita, when a monk accepts alms from a frightened layman.

2\. Mrakshita, when the food is soiled (kharaṇṭita) by animate or inanimate matter.

3\. Nikshipta, when the food is placed among animate things.

4\. Pihita, when animate food is covered with inanimate matter, and vice versa.

5\. Saṃhṛta, when the layman has to take out the thing to be given from one vessel and puts it into another.

6\. Dāyaka, when the condition or occupation of the giver forbids accepting alms from him.

7\. Unmiśrita, when the layman mixes up pure with impure food.

8\. Aparita (?), when one joint possessor gives away from the store against the other's will.

9\. Lipta, when the layman gives food, &c., with a ladle or his hand, soiled with milk, butter, &c.

10\. Chardita, when in giving alms he spills milk, &c.

[pg 134]

There are four faults of paribhōgaishaṇā:

1\. Saṃyōjanā, when the monk puts together the ingredients for a good meal.

2\. Apramāṇa, when he accepts a greater than the prescribed quantity of food.

3\. Iṅgāla, when he praises a rich man for his good fare, or dhūma, when he blames a poor man for his bad fare.

4\. Akāraṇa, when he eats choice food on other occasions than those laid down in the sacred texts.

[^338]: See next page. [pg 135] Aughika and aupagrahika. The former is explained sāmudāyika, the other denotes such things as are wanted occasionally only, as a stick. I cannot make out with certainty from the commentaries whether the broom is reckoned among the former or the latter.

[^339]: This means, according to the commentator, either in taking up or putting down, or with respect to the ōgha and aupagrahika outfit, or with respect to substance and condition of mind.

[^340]: This verse, which is in a different metre (Āryā), is apparently a later addition, and has probably been taken from an old commentary, the Cūrṇi or the Bhāshya.

[^341]: Ajjhusirē = asushirē, not perforated, not having holes. I translate according to the author of the Avacūri. The literal [pg 135] translation would give a wrong idea, as it would come to the same as the word bilavarjita in the next verse.

[^342]: I.e. where the ground has been cleared not long ago by burning the grass, &c.

[^343]: Ōgāḍhē, where the animate ground is covered by at least five digits of inanimate matter.

[^344]: Viz. of mind (20, 21), of speech (22, 23), and of the body 

[^345]: See part i, p. 150, note 2.

[^346]: Saṃrambha.

[^347]: Samārambha.

[^348]: Ārambha.

* * *

## TWENTY-FIFTH LECTURE. THE TRUE SACRIFICE.

There was a famous Brāhmaṇa, Jayaghōsha by name, who was born in a Brāhmanical family, but who was pledged to the performing of the yamas [^349].  {#25:1}

This great sage, who subdued all his senses, and who walked on the right road, came, on his wandering from village to village, to the town of Benares.  {#25:2}

There outside of Benares he took up his lodgings in a pleasant park; there he took up his abode in a pure place to live and sleep in.  {#25:3}

At the same time a Brāhmaṇa, versed in the Vēdas, Vijayaghōsha by name, offered a sacrifice in that town.  {#25:4}

Now this houseless (monk) at the end of a fast of a month's duration, went to the sacrifice of Vijayaghōsha to beg alms.  {#25:5}

[pg 137]

The priest wanted to turn the approaching monk off: 'I shall not give you alms, mendicant, beg somewhere else.  {#25:6}

'Priests who are versed in the Vēdas and are chaste as behoves offerers, who are versed in the Jyōtishāṅga [^350] and are well grounded in the sacrificial science, who are able to save themselves. and others, such priests ought to be presented with food and all they desire.'  {#25:7}

When the great sage was thus refused by the priest, he was neither angry nor pleased, as he always strove for the highest good.  {#25:9}

Not to obtain food, or drink, or whatever else he wanted, but to save these people he spoke the following words:  {#25:10}

"You do not know what is most essential [^351] in the Vēdas, nor in sacrifices, nor in the heavenly bodies [^352], nor in duties [^353].  {#25:11}

"Nor do you know those who are able to save themselves and others; but if you do, then speak out!"  {#25:12}

The priest did not make a reply to defend himself against his insinuation; but he and all there assembled joined their hands and questioned the great sage:  {#25:13}

'Tell us the most essential subject in the Vēdas, and tell us what is most essential in the sacrifice; [pg 138] tell us the first of the heavenly bodies, and tell us the best of dharmas.  {#25:14}

'Who are able to save themselves and others (viz. tell me). I ask you to solve this my doubt, O saint.'  {#25:15}

"The most essential subject in the Vēdas is the agnihōtra, and that of the sacrifice is the purpose of the sacrifice [^354]; the first of the heavenly bodies is the moon, and the best of dharmas is that of Kāśyapa (i.e. Ṛshabha).  {#25:16}

"The beautiful (gods) with joined hands praise and worship the highest Lord (i.e. the Tīrthakara) as the planets, &c., (praise) the moon.  {#25:17}

"The ignorant (priests) pretend to know the sacrifice, those whose Brāhmanical excellence consists in (false) science; they shroud themselves in study and penance, being like fire covered by ashes.  {#25:18}

"He who is called by people a Brāhmaṇa and is worshipped like fire (is no true Brāhmaṇa). But him we call a true Brāhmaṇa, whom the wise point out as such.  {#25:19}

"He who has no worldly attachment after entering the order, who does not repent of having become a monk [^355], and who takes delight in the noble words, him we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:20}

"He who is exempt from love, hatred, and fear, [pg 139] (and who shines forth) like burnished gold, purified in fire [^356], him we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:21}

"A lean, self-subduing ascetic, who reduces his flesh and blood, who is pious and has reached Nirvāṇa, him we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:22}

"He who thoroughly knows living beings, whether they move or not, and does not injure them in any of the three ways [^357], him we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:23}

"He who does not speak untruth from anger or for fun, from greed or from fear, him we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:24}

"He who does not take anything that is not given him, be it sentient or not sentient, small or large, him we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:25}

"He who does not carnally love divine, human, or animal beings, in thoughts, words, or acts, him we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:26}

"He who is not defiled by pleasures as a lotus growing in the water is not wetted by it, him we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:27}

"He who is not greedy, who lives unknown, who has no house and no property, and who has no friendship with householders, him we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:28}

"He who has given up his former connections (with his parents, &c.), with his kinsmen and relations, and who is not given to pleasure, him we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:29}

[pg 140]

"The binding of animals (to the sacrificial pole), all the Vēdas, and sacrifices, being causes of sin, cannot save the sinner; for his works (or Karman) are very powerful.  {#25:30}

"One does not become a Śramaṇa by the tonsure, nor a Brāhmaṇa by the sacred syllable ōṃ, nor a Muni by living in the woods, nor a Tāpasa by wearing (clothes of) Kuśa-grass and bark.  {#25:31}

"One becomes a Śramaṇa by equanimity, a Brāhmaṇa by chastity, a Muni by knowledge, and a Tāpasa by penance.  {#25:32}

"By one's actions one becomes a Brāhmaṇa, or a Kshattriya, or a Vaiśya, or a Śūdra.  {#25:33}

"The Enlightened One has declared these (good qualities) through which one becomes a (true) Snātaka [^358]; him who is exempt from all Karman, we call a Brāhmaṇa.  {#25:34}

"The most excellent twice-born men [^359] who possess these good qualities, are able to save themselves and others."  {#25:35}

When thus his doubt had been solved, Vijayaghōsha, the Brāhmaṇa, assented [^360] to the great sage Jayaghōsha and to his (speech).  {#25:36}

Vijayaghōsha, pleased, folded his hands and spoke [pg 141] as follows: 'You have well declared to me what true Brāhmaṇahood consists in.  {#25:37}

'You are a sacrificer of sacrifices, you are the most learned of those who know the Vēdas, you know the Jyōtishāṅga, you know perfectly the Law.  {#25:38}

'You are able to save yourself and others; therefore do us the honour to accept our alms, O best of monks.'  {#25:39}

"I do not want any alms; but, O Brāhmaṇa, enter the order at once, lest you should be drifted about on the dreadful ocean of the Saṃsāra, whose eddies are dangers.  {#25:40}

"There is glue (as it were) in pleasure: those who are not given to pleasure, are not soiled by it; those who love pleasures, must wander about in the Saṃsāra; those who do not, will be liberated.  {#25:41}

"If you take two clods of clay, one wet, the other dry, and fling them against the wall, the wet one will stick to it.  {#25:42}

"Thus foolish men, who love pleasure, will be fastened (to Karman), but the passionless will not, even as the dry clod of clay (does not stick to the wall)."  {#25:43}

When Vijayaghōsha had learned the excellent Law from the houseless Jayaghōsha, he entered the order.  {#25:44}

Jayaghōsha and Vijayaghōsha both annihilated their Karman by self-control and penance, and reached the highest perfection.  {#25:45}

Thus I say.

[^349]: Yama etymologically means 'restraint;' here it denotes the great vows of the Jainas; cf. XXIII, 12, p. 121 and note 2.

[^350]: It is worthy of note that, according to the opinion of our author, the knowledge of astronomy, as taught in the Jyōtisha, was one Of the principal accomplishments of a priest. This quality of a priest must therefore have been more conspicuous to an outsider than Brāhmanical books would make us believe.

[^351]: To render muha = mukha.

[^352]: Nakshatra.

[^353]: Dharma.

[^354]: Jannaṭṭhī vēyasā muham = yajñārthī vedasām mukham. According to the Dīpikā sacrifice here means the ten virtues: truth, penance, content, patience, right conduct, simplicity, faith, constancy, not injuring anything, and Saṃvara.

[^355]: According to the commentators we should translate: He who does not embrace (his people) on meeting them, and is not sorry on leaving them.

[^356]: Niddhantamalapāvagaṃ. The commentator assumes a transposition of the members in this compound. Such irregular compounds are not unfrequent in our Prākṛt. If, however, pāvaga stands for pāpaka, the compound would be regular, and would refer not to 'gold,' but to the person described. In that case we must translate: whose impurities and sins had been annihilated.

[^357]: I.e. by thoughts, words, and acts.

[^358]: Snātaka denotes a Brahman who has finished his studies; it here means as much as 'a perfect sage.'

[^359]: A various reading in one MS. adds, 'Jinas and Brāhmaṇas' before dvijas.

[^360]: Samudāya tayaṃ taṃ tu. The text is evidently corrupted. Samudāya stands, according to the commentators, for samādāya, but there is no finite verb with which to construe the absolute participle, either expressed or easily supplies'. Perhaps we must read samuvāya vayaṃ = samuvāca vacas; for the perfect is retained in some cases. However, if this conjecture be right, the next line would be superfluous.

* * *

[pg 142]

## TWENTY-SIXTH LECTURE. THE CORRECT BEHAVIOUR.

I shall declare the correct behaviour (sāmācārī) which causes freedom from all misery; by practising it the Nirgranthas have crossed the ocean of Saṃsāra.  {#26:1}

The correct behaviour of monks consists of (the following) ten parts: 1. āvaśyikā; 2. naishēdhikī; 3. āpṛcchanā; 4. pratipṛcchanā; 5. chandanā; 6. icchākāra; 7. mithyākāra; 8. tathākāra; 9. abhyutthāna; 10. upasampad.  {#26:2}

The āvaśyikā is required when he leaves a room (or the presence of other monks on some necessary business); the naishēdhikī, on entering a place; āpṛcchanā, (or asking the superior's permission) for what he is to do himself; pratipṛcchanā, for what somebody else is to do; chandanā, (or placing at the disposal of other monks) the things one has got; icchākāra, in the execution (of one's intention by oneself or somebody else); mithyākāra, in the blaming oneself (for sins committed); tathākāra, (assent) in making a promise; abhyutthāna, in serving those who deserve respect; and upasampad, in placing oneself under another teacher. Thus the twice fivefold behaviour has been declared.  {#26:5}

After sunrise during the first quarter (of the first Paurushī) [^361] he should inspect (and clean) his things and pay his respects to the superior.  {#26:8}

Then, with his hands joined, he should ask him: [pg 143] 'What shall I do now? I want to be employed, sir, in doing some work or in studying.'  {#26:9}

If he is ordered to do some work, he should do it without tiring; if he is ordered to study, he should do it without allowing himself to be affected by any pains.  {#26:10}

A clever monk should divide the day into four (equal) parts (called paurushī), and fulfil his duties (uttaraguṇa) in all four parts.  {#26:11}

In the first Paurushī he should study, in the second he should meditate, in the third he should go on his begging-tour, and in the fourth he should study again.  {#26:12}

In the month Āshāḍha the Paurushī (of the night) contains two feet (pada) [^362]; in the month Pausha, four; in the months Caitra and Aśvayuja, three.  {#26:13}

(The Paurushī) increases or decreases a digit [^363] (aṅgula) every week, two digits every fortnight, four digits every month.  {#26:14}

The dark fortnight of Āshāḍha, Bhādrapada, Kārttika, Pausha, Phālguna, and Vaiśākha are known as avamarātrās [^364].  {#26:15}

[pg 144]

In the quarter of the year comprising the three months Jyēshṭhāmūla, Āshāḍha, and Śrāvaṇa, the (morning-) inspection is to last six digits (beyond ¼ Paurushī); in the second quarter, eight; in the third, ten; in the fourth, eight [^365].  {#26:16}

A clever monk should divide the night too into four parts, and fulfil his duties (uttaraguṇa) in all four parts.  {#26:17}

In the first Paurushī he should study, in the second he should meditate, in the third he should leave off sleep, and in the fourth he should study again. 08) {#26:19}

When the nakshatra which leads the night [^366] has reached the first quarter of the heaven, at dawn he should cease to study. 

When a small part of the quarter is left [^367], in which the (leading) nakshatra stands, during that space of time, being considered intermediate [^368] (between two) days, a monk should watch.  {#26:20}

In the first quarter (of the first Paurushī) he should inspect (and clean) his things, pay his respects to his superior, and then begin to study, not allowing himself to be affected by any pains [^369].  {#26:21}

[pg 145]

In the (last) quarter of the first Paurushī, after paying his respect to the Guru, a monk should inspect his almsbowl, without, however, performing the Kāla-pratikramaṇa [^370].  {#26:22}

He should first inspect his mouth-cloth [^371], then his broom [^372], and taking the broom in his hand he should inspect his cloth.  {#26:23}

Standing upright he holds his cloth firmly and inspects it first leisurely, then he spreads it, and at last he wipes it.  {#26:24}

(He should spread the cloth) without shaking or crushing it, in such a way as to make the folds disappear, and to avoid friction of its parts against each other; he should fold it up six times in length, and nine times in breadth, and then he should remove living beings with his hand (spreading the cloth on the palm of his hand) [^373].  {#26:25}

He must avoid want of attention: 1. in beginning his work; 2. in taking up the corners of the cloth; 3. in folding it up; 4. in shaking out the dust; 5. in putting it down (on some other piece of cloth); 6. in sitting upon the haunches [^374].  {#26:26}

[pg 146]

(One must further avoid) to hold the cloth loosely, or at one corner, or so as to let it flap, or so as to subject it to friction, or so as to shake it in different ways, or if one has made a mistake in the number of foldings (see verse 25) to count (aloud or with the help of the fingers, &c.) [^375]  {#26:27}

There should be neither too little nor too much of inspection, nor an exchange (of the things to be inspected); this is the right way to do (the inspection), all other methods are wrong:--  {#26:28}

(This is) if one engaged in inspecting his things converses or gossips (with anybody), renounces something [^376], teaches another his lesson, or receives his own lesson from another, (he neglects his inspection).  {#26:29}

He who is careful in the inspection, protects the six kinds of living beings, viz. the earth-bodies, water-bodies, fire-bodies, wind-bodies, plants, and animals.  {#26:30}

He who is careless in the inspection, injures the six kinds of living beings (just enumerated) [^377].  {#26:31}

In the third Paurushī he should beg food and drink, (he may do so) for any of the following six reasons:  {#26:32}

[pg 147]

1\. To prevent an illness; 2. to serve the Guru; 3. to be able to comply with the rules about walking [^378]; 4\. to be able to comply with the rules of self-control [^379]; 5\. to save one's life; 6. to be able to meditate on the Law.  {#26:33}

A zealous Nirgrantha or Nirgranthī may omit to beg food for the following six reasons, when it will not be considered a transgression of his duties:  {#26:34}

1\. In case of illness; 2. in case of a disaster; 3. to preserve one's chastity and the Guptis; 4. out of compassion for living beings; 5. in the interest of penance; 6. to make an end of one's life [^380].  {#26:35}

Taking his whole outfit a monk should inspect it with his eye; he then may walk about, but not beyond half a Yōjana.  {#26:36}

In the fourth Paurushī he should put away his almsbowl (after having eaten his meal), and then begins his study which reveals all existent things.  {#26:37}

In the last quarter of the fourth Paurushī he should pay his reverence to the Guru, and after having performed Kāla-pratikramaṇa [^381], he should inspect his lodging.  {#26:38}

A zealous monk should also inspect the place where to discharge his excrements and urine, and then (till the sun sets) he should go through Kāyōtsarga without allowing himself to be affected by any pains.  {#26:39}

[pg 148]

Then he should, in due order, reflect on all transgressions he has committed during the day, with regard to knowledge, faith, and conduct.  {#26:40}

Having finished Kāyōtsarga, and paid his reverence to the Guru, he should, in due order, confess his transgressions committed during the day.  {#26:41}

Then having recited the Pratikramaṇa Sūtra [^382], and having annihilated his sins, he should pay his reverence to the Guru (asking absolution) [^383], and go through Kāyōtsarga without allowing himself to be affected by any pains.  {#26:42}

Having finished Kāyōtsarga, and paid his reverence to the Guru, he should pronounce the customary (three) praises, and then wait for the proper time.  {#26:43}

In the first Paurushī (of the night) he should study; in the second he should meditate; in the third he should leave off sleep; and in the fourth he should study again [^384].  {#26:44}

In the fourth Paurushī he should wait for the proper time and then begin to study without waking the householders.  {#26:45}

In the last quarter of the fourth Paurushī he should pay his reverence to the Guru, and performing Kāla-pratikramaṇa [^385] he should wait for the proper time.  {#26:46}

When the (time for) Kāyōtsarga has arrived, he [pg 149] should go through it, without allowing himself to be affected by any pains.  {#26:47}

Then he should, in due order, reflect on all transgressions he has committed during the night with regard to knowledge, faith, and conduct.  {#26:48}

Having finished Kāyōtsarga and paid his reverence to the Guru, he should, in due order, confess his transgressions committed during the night.  {#26:49}

Then having recited the Pratikramaṇa Sūtra &c. (see verse 41).  {#26:50}

He should consider what kind of austerities he will undertake. Having finished his Kāyōtsarga, he pays his reverence to the Guru.  {#26:51}

Having finished Kāyōtsarga and paid his reverence to the Guru, he should practise those austerities which he has decided upon, and praise the perfected saints.  {#26:52}

Thus has been summarily declared the correct behaviour, by practising which many souls have crossed the ocean of Saṃsāra.  {#26:53}

Thus I say.

[^361]: The southern half of the sky or horizon, between east and west, is divided into four quarters, each of which corresponds in time to a paurushī, the fourth part of a day or a night.

[^362]: A paurushī is the fourth part of a day or a night; about the time of the equinoxes, when the day as well as the night contains twelve hours, the paurushī contains three hours. At the same time, in the months Kaitra and Āśvina, as we learn from our verse, the paurushī has three feet, padas. The pada therefore is equal to one hour exactly. The duration of the night at the summer solstice was therefore estimated at eight hours and at the winter solstice at sixteen, just as in the Vedic Jyōtisha.

[^363]: A digit, aṅgula, is apparently the twelfth part of a foot, pada = one hour. The digit is therefore equal to five minutes.

[^364]: The fortnights, mentioned in the text, consist of fourteen days only, the remaining ones of fifteen days. In this way the lunar year is made to consist of 354 days.

[^365]: Or thirty, forty, fifty, forty minutes respectively.

[^366]: I.e. the nakshatra which is in opposition to the sun, and accordingly rises at the same time with the setting sun, and sets with the rising sun, compare Rāmāyaṇa III, 16, 12.

[^367]: I.e. is about to set.

[^368]: Vērattiya, translated vairātrika; but there is no such word in Sanskrit. It apparently stands for dvairātrika, belonging to two days. As the Hindus reckon the day from sunrise, the time immediately preceding it may be considered to belong to two days.

[^369]: Compare verse 8.

[^370]: I.e. expiation of sins concerning time, cf. Bhandarkar's Report, p. 98, note ‡. It seems to consist in Kāyōtsarga.

[^371]: This is a piece of muslin which the Jaina monks place before their mouth in speaking, in order to prevent insects being drawn in the mouth by the breath.

[^372]: It is here called gōcchaga = gucchaka, originally a bunch of peacocks' feathers, it is so still, if I am not mistaken, with the Digambaras, whilst the Śvētāmbaras use other materials, especially cotton threads.

[^373]: Much in my translation is conjectural. There are some technicalities in these verses which I fail to understand clearly, notwithstanding the explanations of the scholiasts.

[^374]: Vēdikā.

[^375]: I am not sure of having hit the true meaning. The commentators reckon this counting as a fault, while the text itself seems to enjoin it.

[^376]: Dēi paccakkhānaṃ. The meaning is, I believe, that during the time of inspection one should not make up one's mind to abstain from this or that because one is to devote one's whole attention to the inspection of one's things.

[^377]: The Dīpikā places this verse before the last and construes it with verse 29, making out the following meaning: if one, engaged in inspecting his things, converses or gossips, &c., then, being careless in the inspection, he injures, &c.
