[^378]: Iriyaṭṭhāē; for one will not be careful about walking (īryā-samiti) if too hungry or thirsty.

[^379]: For one might eat forbidden food if too hungry.

[^380]: It may be remarked here that the verses 25, 16, 19, 20, 24, 26, 27, 29, 33, 34, 35 are in the Āryā-metre while the rest of the lecture is in Slōka.

[^381]: Compare note [^370], .

[^382]: Paḍikamittu = pratikramya, explained pratikramaṇasūtram uktvā.

[^383]: According to the Dīpikā: having repeated the three Gāthās beginning āriyauvajjhāya.

[^384]: This verse is the same as verse 18, except a verbal difference in the last line.

[^385]: Paḍikamittu kālassa, see , note [^370]. The Dīpikā here explains this phrase by: doing acts proper for that time.

* * *

## TWENTY-SEVENTH LECTURE. THE BAD BULLOCKS.

There was a Sthavira and Gaṇadhara [^386], the learned sage Garga. This leader of the Gaṇa once made the following reflections:  {#27:1}

'He who rides in a car, crosses a wilderness; he [pg 150] who rides, as it were, in (the car of) religious exercise, crosses the Saṃsāra.  {#27:2}

'But he who puts bad bullocks [^387] before his car, will be tired out with beating them; he will feel vexation, and his goad will be broken (at last).  {#27:3}

'(A bad bullock) will bite its mate in the tail; it will wound the other [^388]; it will break the pin of the yoke [^389], or it will leave the road.  {#27:4}

'It will fall down on its side, or sit down, or lie down; it will jump up or caper, or it will obstinately make for a young cow.  {#27:5}

'It will furiously advance with its head lowered for an attack, or angrily go backward; it will stand still as if dead, or run at full speed.  {#27:6}

'The cursed beast [^390] will rend asunder the rope, or in its unruliness break the yoke; and roaring it will break loose and run off.  {#27:7}

'Just as bad bullocks are when put before a car, so are bad pupils when yoked, as it were, to the car of the Law; they break down through want of zeal.  {#27:8}

'Some attach great importance [^391] to their success; [pg 151] some to their good fare; some to their comfort; some nurse their anger.  {#27:9}

'Some are averse to begging; some are afraid of insults and are stuck up; (how can) I convince them by reasons and arguments [^392] (?)  {#27:10}

'(A bad pupil) makes objections, and points out (imagined) difficulties; he frequently acts in opposition to the words of the superiors.  {#27:11}

'(He will say if sent to a lady): "She does not know me, she will give me nothing; I suppose she will be gone out; send some other monk there."  {#27:12}

'If sent on an errand, they do not do what they were bidden [^393], but stroll about wherever they like; or deporting themselves like servants of the king [^394], they knit their brows (when speaking to other people).  {#27:13}

'After they have been instructed, admitted into the order, and nourished with food and drink, they disperse in all directions like geese whose wings have grown.'  {#27:14}

Now this driver (viz. Garga), who had to deal with bad bullocks, thought: 'What have I to do with bad pupils? I am disheartened.  {#27:15}

'As are bad pupils, so are bad bullocks; I shall leave these lazy donkeys, and shall practise severe austerities.' [pg 152] =17= That noble man, who was full of kindness, grave, and always meditating, wandered about on the earth, leading a virtuous life.  {#27:16}

Thus I say.

[^386]: Gaṇa seems to correspond to the modern Gaccha; see part i, p. 288, note 2. Gaṇadhara, therefore, does not denote here, as usual, a disciple of Tīrthakara.

[^387]: Khaluṃka = galivṛshabha. Gali is explained in the dictionaries: a strong but lazy bull. In verse 16 we meet with galigaddaha = galigardabha, as synonymous with khaluṃka.

[^388]: The commentator understands the first line of this verse as having reference to the angry driver. But though an angry driver will perhaps, for all I know, put his bullock's tail to his teeth, still it is harder to supply another subject in the first line than in the second, and in the following verses.

[^389]: Samilā = yugarandhrakīlaka, Avacūri.

[^390]: Chimāla = jāra, see Hēmacandra, Dēśī Kōsha 3, 27. It is a coarse term, which I replace by another, though probably the language of our coach-drivers might supply us with a more idiomatic rendering.

[^391]: Gārava, cf. p. 98, note [^257].

[^392]: The metre of this verse seems to have originally been Āryā, but an attempt has been made to change it into Anushṭubh. We meet here with the interesting form aṇusāsammī (read aṇusasammi) = anuśāsmi.

[^393]: This seems to be the meaning of the word paliuṃcanti. The commentators say, after other explanations, that they pretend not to have met the person to whom they were sent.

[^394]: Rājaveṭṭhiṃ va mannantā; veṭṭhi = vishṭi, hire.

* * *

## TWENTY-EIGHTH LECTURE. THE ROAD TO FINAL DELIVERANCE.

Learn the true road leading to final deliverance, which the Jinas have taught; it depends on four causes and is characterised by right knowledge and faith.  {#28:1}

I. Right knowledge; II. Faith; III. Conduct; and IV. Austerities; this is the road taught by the Jinas who possess the best knowledge.  {#28:2}

Right knowledge, faith, conduct, and austerities; beings who follow this road, will obtain beatitude.  {#28:3}

I. Knowledge is fivefold: 1. Śruta, knowledge derived from the sacred books; 2. Ābhinibōdhika, perception [^395]; 3\. Avadhi, supernatural knowledge; 4. Manaḥparyāya [^396], knowledge of the thoughts of other people; 5. Kēvala, the highest, unlimited knowledge.  {#28:4}

[pg 153]

This is the fivefold knowledge. The wise ones have taught the knowledge of substances, qualities, and all developments [^397].  {#28:5}

Substance is the substrate of qualities; the qualities are inherent in one substance; but the characteristic of developments is that they inhere in either (viz. substances or qualities).  {#28:6}

Dharma, Adharma, space, time, matter, and souls (are the six kinds of substances [^398]); they make up this world, as has been taught by the Jinas who possess the best knowledge.  {#28:7}

Dharma, Adharma, and space are each one substance only; but time, matter, and souls are an infinite number of substances.  {#28:8}

The characteristic of Dharma is motion, that of Adharma immobility, and that of space [^399], which contains all other substances, is to make room (for everything) [^400].  {#28:9}

The characteristic of time is duration [^401], that of soul the realisation [^402] of knowledge, faith, happiness, and misery.  {#28:10}

The characteristic of Soul is knowledge, faith, conduct, austerities, energy, and realisation (of its developments).  {#28:11}

The characteristic of matter is sound, darkness, [pg 154] lustre (of jewels, &c.), light, shade, sunshine; colour, taste, smell, and touch.  {#28:12}

The characteristic of development is singleness, separateness [^403], number, form, conjunction, and disjunction.  {#28:13}

1\. jīva, Soul; 2. ajīva, the inanimate things; 3. bandha, the binding of the soul by Karman; 4. puṇya, merit; 5. pāpa, demerit; 6. āsrava, that which causes the soul to be affected by sins; 7. saṃvara, the prevention of āsrava by watchfulness; 8. the annihilation of Karman; 9. final deliverance: these are the nine truths (or categories).  {#28:14}

He who verily believes the true teaching of the (above nine) fundamental truths, possesses righteousness.  {#28:15}

II. Faith is produced by 1. nisarga, nature; 2. upadēśa, instruction; 3. ājñā, command; 4. sūtra, study of the sūtras; 5. bīja, suggestion; 6. abhigama, comprehension of the meaning of the sacred lore; 7. vistāra, complete course of study; 8. kriyā, religious exercise; 9. saṃkshēpa, brief exposition; 10. dharma, the Law.  {#28:16}

1\. He who truly comprehends, by a spontaneous effort of his mind [^404], (the nature of) soul, inanimate things, merit, and demerit, and who puts an end to sinful influences [^405], (believes by) nature.  {#28:17}

He who spontaneously believes the four truths (explicitly mentioned in the last verse), which the [pg 155] Jinas have taught, (thinking) they are of this and not of a different nature, believes by nature.  {#28:18}

2\. But he who believes these truths, having learned them from somebody else, either a Chadmastha [^406] or a Jina, believes by instruction.  {#28:19}

3\. He who has got rid of love, hate, delusion, and ignorance, and believes because he is told to do so, believes by command.  {#28:20}

4\. He who obtains righteousness by (the study of) the Sūtras, either Aṅgas or other works [^407], believes by the study of Sūtras.  {#28:21}

5\. He who by correctly comprehending one truth arrives at the comprehension of more -- just as a drop of oil expands on the surface of water--believes by suggestion.  {#28:22}

6\. He who truly knows the sacred lore, viz. the eleven Aṅgas, the Prakīrṇas [^408], and the Dṛshṭivāda, believes by the comprehension of the sacred lore.  {#28:23}

7\. He who understands the true nature of all substances by means of all proofs (pramāṇa) and nayas [^409], believes by a complete course of study.  {#28:24}

8\. He who sincerely performs (all duties implied)

[pg 156]

by right knowledge, faith, and conduct, by asceticism and discipline, and by all Samitis and Guptis, believes by religious exercise.  {#28:25}

9\. He who though not versed in the sacred doctrines [^410] nor acquainted with other systems [^411], holds no wrong doctrines, believes by brief exposition.  {#28:26}

10\. He who believes in the truth [^412] of the realities [^413], the Sūtras, and conduct, as it has been explained by the Jinas, believes by the Law.  {#28:27}

Right belief depends on the acquaintance with truth [^414], on the devotion to those who know the truth, and on the avoiding of schismatical and heretical tenets.  {#28:28}

There is no (right) conduct without right belief [^415], and it must be cultivated (for obtaining) right faith; righteousness and conduct originate together, or righteousness precedes (conduct).  {#28:29}

Without (right) faith there is no (right) knowledge, without (right) knowledge there is no virtuous conduct [^416], without virtues there is no deliverance [^417], and without deliverance there is no perfection.  {#28:30}

(The excellence of faith depends on the following) eight points: 1. that one has no doubts (about the truth of the tenets); 2. that one has no preference (for heterodox tenets); 3. that one does not doubt [pg 157] its saving qualities [^418]; 4\. that one is not shaken in the right belief (because heretical sects are more prosperous); 5. that one praises (the pious); 6. that one encourages (weak brethren); 7. that one supports or loves the confessors of the Law; 8. that one endeavours to exalt it.  {#28:31}

III. Conduct, which produces the destruction of all Karman, is 1. sāmāyika [^419], the avoidance of everything sinful; 2. chēdōpasthāpana, the initiation of a novice; 3. parihāraviśuddhika, purity produced by peculiar austerities [^420]; 4\. sūkshma samparāya, reduction of desire; 5. akashāya yathākhyāta, annihilation of sinfulness according to the precepts of the Arhats, as well in the case of a Chadmastha as of a Jina.  {#28:32}

IV. Austerities are twofold: external and internal; both external and internal austerities are sixfold. (34) By knowledge one knows things, by faith one believes in them, by conduct one gets (freedom from Karman), and by austerities one reaches purity.  {#28:35}

Having by control and austerities destroyed their Karman, great sages, whose purpose is to get rid of all misery, proceed to (perfection).

Thus I say.

[^395]: This is usually called mati, and is placed before śruta. The same enumeration recurs in XXXIII, 4, p. 193. Umāsvāti in Mōksha Sūtra I, 14, gives the following synonyms of mati: smṛti, cintā, abhinibōdha.

[^396]: Maṇanāṇaṃ.

[^397]: Dravya, guṇa, paryāya (pajjava in Jaina Prākṛt). Guṇa, quality, is generally not admitted by the Jainas as a separate category, see Śīlāṅka's refutation of the Vaiśēshika doctrines at the end of his comments on Sūtrakṛtāṅga I, 12 (Bombay edition, p. 482).

[^398]: They are frequently called astikāyas, or realities.

[^399]: It is here called nabhas instead of ākāśa.

[^400]: Avagāha.

[^401]: Vartanā.

[^402]: Upayōga.

[^403]: Singleness (ēkatva) makes a thing appear as one thing, separateness (pṛthaktva) as different from others.

[^404]: Sahasamuiya = svayaṃsamudita. It is usually rendered sahasammati.

[^405]: Āsravasaṃvara, see above, verse 14, 6 and 7.

[^406]: A chadmastha is one who has not yet obtained Kēvala, or the highest knowledge; he is in the two guṇasthānas (the fourteen stages in the development of the soul from the lowest to the highest) characterised as 1. upaśāntamōha, and 2. kshīṇamōha; viz. 1. that in which delusion is only temporarily separated from the soul, and 2. that in which delusion is finally destroyed.

[^407]: Bāhira; apparently the same works are intended which are elsewhere called anaṅgapravishṭa.

[^408]: The original has the singular.

[^409]: The seven nayas are 'points of view or principles with reference to which certain judgments are arrived at or arrangements made.' Bhandarkar, Report, p. 112.

[^410]: Pravacana.

[^411]: E. g. that of Kapila, &c., Comm.

[^412]: Dharma.

[^413]: Astikāya; see note on verse 7.

[^414]: I.e. true things as soul, &c.

[^415]: Saṃyaktva righteousness.'

[^416]: Caraṇaguṇa. The commentators make this a dvandva compound, and interpret caraṇa as vratādi, and guṇa as piṇḍaviśuddhi, &c.

[^417]: By deliverance I have rendered mōksha, and by final perfection nirvāṇa. Mōksha denotes freedom from Karman, a condition which in Brāhmanical philosophy is called jīvanmukti.

[^418]: Nivvitigicchā = nirvicikitsa. According to the commentary it may stand for nir-vid-jugupsā 'without loathing the saints.'

[^419]: See Bhandarkar, Report, p. 98, note ‡.

[^420]: The Dīpikā contains the following details. Nine monks resolve to live together for eighteen months. They make one of their number their superior, kalpasthita, four become parihārikas, and the remaining four serve them (anuparihārikas). After six months the parihārikas become anuparihārikas and vice versa. After another six months the kalpasthita does penance and all the other monks serve him as anuparihārikas.

* * *

[pg 158]

## TWENTY-NINTH LECTURE. THE EXERTION IN RIGHTEOUSNESS.

O long-lived (Jambūsvāmin)! I (Sudharman) have heard the following discourse from the venerable (Mahāvīra). {#29:1}

Here, forsooth, the Venerable Ascetic Mahāvīra, of the Kaśyapa Gōtra, has delivered this lecture called the exertion in righteousness. Many creatures, who truly believe in the subject (taught in this lecture), put their faith in it, give credence to it, accept it, practise it, comply with it, study it, understand it, learn it, and act up to it according to the precept (of the Jinas) [^421]--have obtained perfection, enlightenment, deliverance, final beatitude, and have put an end to all misery.

This lecture treats of the following subjects:

1\. saṃvēga, longing for liberation;

2\. nirvēda, disregard of worldly objects;

3\. dharmaśraddhā, desire of the Law;

4\. gurusādharmikaśuśrūshaṇā, obedience to co-religionists and to the Guru.

5\. ālōcanā, confession of sins before the Guru;

6\. nindā, repenting of one's sins to oneself;

7\. garhā, repenting of one's sins before the Guru; [pg 159] 8\. sāmāyika, moral and intellectual purity of the soul;

9\. caturviṃśatistava, adoration of the twenty-four Jinas;

10\. vandana, paying reverence to the Guru;

11\. pratikramaṇa, expiation of sins;

12\. kāyōtsarga, a particular position of the body;

13\. pratyākhyāna, self-denial;

14\. stavastutimaṅgala, praises and hymns;

15\. kālasya pratyupēkshaṇā, keeping the right time;

16\. prāyaścittakaraṇa, practising penance;

17\. kshamāpana, begging forgiveness;

18\. svādhyāya, study;

19\. vācanā, recital of the sacred texts;

20\. paripṛcchanā, questioning (the teacher);

21\. parāvartanā, repetition;

22\. anuprēkshā, pondering;

23\. dharmakathā, religious discourse;

24\. śrutasyārādhanā, acquisition of sacred knowledge;

25\. ēkāgramanaḥsannivēśanā, concentration of thoughts;

26\. saṃyama, control;

27\. tapas, austerities;

28\. vyavadāna, cutting off the Karman;

29\. sukhāśāta, renouncing pleasure;

30\. apratibaddhatā, mental independence;

31\. vicitraśayanāsanasēvanā, using unfrequented lodgings and beds;

32\. vinivartanā, turning from the world;

33\. sambhōgapratyākhyāna, renouncing collection of alms in one district only; [pg 160] 34\. upadhipratyākhyāna, renouncing articles of use;

35\. āhārapratyākhyāna, renouncing food;

36\. kashāyapratyākhyāna, conquering the passions;

37\. yōgapratyākhyāna, renouncing activity;

38\. śarīrapratyākhyāna, renouncing the body;

39\. sahāyapratyākhyāna, renouncing company;

40\. bhaktapratyākhyāna, renouncing all food;

41\. sadbhāvapratyākhyāna, perfect renunciation;

42\. pratirūpatā, conforming to the standard;

43\. vaiyāvṛtya, doing service;

44\. sarvaguṇasampūrṇatā, fulfilling all virtues;

45\. vītarāgatā, freedom from passion;

46\. kshānti, patience;

47\. mukti, freedom from greed;

48, ārjava, simplicity;

49\. mārdava, humility;

50\. bhāvasatya, sincerity of mind;

51\. karaṇasatya, sincerity of religious practice;

52\. yōgasatya, sincerity of acting;

53\. manōguptatā, watchfulness of the mind;

54\. vāg-guptatā, watchfulness of the speech;

55\. kāyaguptatā, watchfulness of the body;

56\. manaḥsamādhāraṇā, discipline of the mind;

57\. vāksamādhāraṇā, discipline of the speech;

58\. kāyasamādhāraṇā, discipline of the body;

59\. jñānasampannatā, possession of knowledge;

60\. darśanasampannatā, possession of faith;

61\. cāritrasampannatā, possession of conduct; [pg 161] 62\. śrōtrēndriyanigraha, subduing the ear;

63\. cakshurindriyanigraha, subduing the eye;

64\. ghrāṇēndriyanigraha, subduing the organ of smell;

65\. jihvēndriyanigraha, subduing the tongue;

66\. sparśanēndriyanigraha, subduing the organ of touch;

67\. krōdhavijaya, conquering anger;

68\. mānavijaya, conquering pride;

69\. māyāvijaya, conquering deceit;

70\. lōbhavijaya, conquering greed;

71\. prēmadvēshamithyādarśaṇavijaya, conquering love, hate, and wrong belief;

72\. śailēśī, stability;

73\. akarmatā, freedom from Karman.

1\. Sir, what does the soul obtain by the longing for liberation? By the longing for liberation the soul obtains an intense desire of the Law; by an intense desire of the Law he quickly arrives at an (increased) longing for liberation; he destroys anger, pride, deceit, and greed, which reproduce themselves infinitely; he acquires no (bad) Karman, and ridding himself of wrong belief which is the consequence of the latter, he becomes possessed of right faith; by the purity of faith some will reach perfection after one birth; nobody, however, who has got this purity, will be born more than thrice before he reaches perfection. 

2\. Sir, what does the soul obtain by disregard of worldly objects [^422]? By disregard of worldly objects the soul quickly feels disgust for pleasures enjoyed by gods, men, and animals; he becomes indifferent to [pg 162] all objects; thereby he ceases to engage in any undertakings, in consequence of which he leaves the road of Saṃsāra and enters the road to perfection.  {#29:2}

3\. Sir [^423], what does the soul obtain by the desire of the Law? By the desire of the Law the soul becomes indifferent to pleasures and happiness to which he was attached; he abandons the life of householders, and as a houseless monk he puts an end to all pains of body and mind, which consist in (the suffering of) cutting, piercing, union (with unpleasant things), &c.; and he obtains unchecked happiness.  {#29:3}

4\. By obedience to co-religionists and to the Guru the soul obtains discipline (vinaya). By discipline and avoidance of misconduct (towards the teacher [^424]) he avoids being reborn as a denizen of hell, an animal, a (low) man, or a (bad) god; by zealous praise of, devotion to, and respect for (the Guru) he obtains birth as a (good) man or god, gains perfection and beatitude, does all praiseworthy actions prescribed by discipline, and prevails upon others to adopt discipline.  {#29:4}

5\. By confession of sins (before the Guru) the soul gets rid of the thorns, as it were, of deceit, misapplied austerities [^425], and wrong belief, which obstruct the way to final liberation and cause an endless migration of the soul; he obtains simplicity, whereby the soul which is free from deceit does not acquire that Karman which results in his having a carnal desire for a woman or eunuch [^426], and annihilates such Karman as he had acquired before.  {#29:5}

[pg 163]

6\. By repenting of one's sins to oneself the soul obtains repentance, and becoming indifferent by repentance he prepares for himself an (ascending) scale of virtues [^427], by which he destroys the Karman resulting from delusion.  {#29:6}

7\. By repenting of one's sins before the Guru the soul obtains humiliation; feeling humiliated, he will leave off all blameable occupations [^428], and apply himself to praiseworthy occupations, whereby a houseless monk will stop infinite disabling [^429] developments.  {#29:7}

8\. By moral and intellectual purity (literally, equilibrium) the soul ceases from sinful occupations.  {#29:8}

9\. By the adoration of the twenty-four Jinas the soul arrives at purity of faith.  {#29:9}

10\. By paying reverence (to the Guru) the soul destroys such Karman as leads to birth in low families, and acquires such Karman as leads to birth in noble families; he wins the affection of people, which results in his being looked upon as an authority, and he brings about general goodwill.  {#29:10}

11\. By expiation of sins he obviates transgressions of the vows; thereby he stops the Āsravas, preserves a pure conduct, practises the eight articles [^430], does not neglect (the practice of control), and pays great attention to it.  {#29:11}

[pg 164]

12\. By Kāyōtsarga he gets rid of past and present (transgressions which require) Prāyaścitta [^431]; thereby his mind is set at ease like a porter who is eased of his burden; and engaging in praiseworthy contemplation he enjoys happiness.  {#29:12}

13\. By self-denial he shuts, as it were, the doors of the Āsravas; by self-denial he prevents desires rising in him; by prevention of desires he becomes, as it were, indifferent and cool towards all objects.  {#29:13}

14\. By praises and hymns he obtains the wisdom consisting in knowledge, faith, and conduct; thereby he gains such improvement, that he will put an end to his worldly existence [^432], (or) be born afterwards in one of the Kalpas and Vimānas [^433].  {#29:14}

15\. By keeping the right time he destroys the Karman which obstructs right knowledge.  {#29:15}

16\. By practising Prāyaścitta [^431] he gets rid of sins, and commits no transgressions; he who correctly practises Prāyaścitta, gains the road and the reward of the road [^434], he wins the reward of good conduct.  {#29:16}

17\. By begging forgiveness he obtains happiness of mind; thereby he acquires a kind disposition towards all kinds of living beings [^435]; by this [pg 165] kind disposition he obtains purity of character and freedom from fear.  {#29:17}

18\. By study he destroys the Karman which obstructs right knowledge.  {#29:18}

19\. By the recital of the sacred texts he obtains destruction of Karman, and contributes to preserve the sacred lore, whereby he acquires the Law of the Tīrtha [^436], which again leads him to the complete destruction of Karman, and to the final annihilation of worldly existence.  {#29:19}

20\. By questioning (the teacher) he arrives at a correct comprehension of the Sūtra and its meaning, and he puts an end to the Karman which produces doubts and delusion.  {#29:20}

21\. By repetition he reproduces the sounds (i.e. syllables) and commits them to memory.  {#29:21}

22\. By pondering (on what he has learned) he loosens the firm hold which the seven kinds of Karman, except the Āyushka [^437] (have upon the soul); he shortens their duration when it was to be a long one; he mitigates their power when it was intense; (he reduces their sphere of action when it was a wide one) [^438]; he may either acquire Āyushka-karman or not, but he no more accumulates Karman which [pg 166] produces unpleasant feelings, and he quickly crosses the very large forest of the fourfold Saṃsāra, which is without beginning and end.  {#29:22}

23\. By religious discourses he obtains destruction of the Karman; by religious discourses he exalts the creed, and by exalting the creed he acquires Karman, which secures, for the future, permanent bliss.  {#29:23}

24\. By acquisition of sacred knowledge he destroys ignorance, and will not be corrupted by worldliness.  {#29:24}

25\. By concentration of his thoughts he obtains stability of the mind.  {#29:25}

26\. By control he obtains freedom from sins.  {#29:26}

27\. By austerities he cuts off the Karman [^439].  {#29:27}

28\. By cutting off the Karman he obtains (the fourth stage of pure meditation characterised by) freedom from actions, by doing no actions he will obtain perfection, enlightenment, deliverance, and final beatitude, and will put an end to all misery.  {#29:28}

29\. By renouncing pleasures he obtains freedom from false longing, whereby he becomes compassionate, humble, free from sorrow, and destroys the Karman produced by delusion regarding conduct.  {#29:29}

30\. By mental independence he gets rid of attachment, whereby he will concentrate his thoughts (on the Law), and will for ever be without attachment and fondness (for worldly things).  {#29:30}

31\. By using unfrequented lodgings and beds he obtains the Gupti of conduct, whereby he will use [pg 167] allowed food, be steady in his conduct, be exclusively delighted with (control), obtain a yearning for deliverance, and cut off the tie of the eightfold Karman.  {#29:31}

32\. By turning from the world he will strive to do no bad actions, and will eliminate his already acquired Karman by its destruction; then he will cross the forest of the fourfold Saṃsāra.  {#29:32}

33\. By renouncing collection of alms in one district only [^440] he overcomes obstacles [^441]; unchecked by them he exerts himself to attain liberation; he is content with the alms he gets, and does not hope for, care for, wish, desire, or covet those of a fellow-monk; not envying other monks he takes up a separate, agreeable lodging [^442].  {#29:33}

34\. By renouncing articles of use [^443] he obtains successful study; without articles of use he becomes exempt from desires, and does not suffer misery.  {#29:34}

35\. By renouncing (forbidden) food he ceases to act for the sustenance of his life; ceasing to act for the sustenance of his life he does not suffer misery when without food.  {#29:35}

36\. By conquering his passions he becomes free from passions; thereby he becomes indifferent to happiness and pains.  {#29:36}

37\. By renouncing activity he obtains inactivity, by ceasing to act he acquires no new Karman, and destroys the Karman he had acquired before.  {#29:37}

38\. By renouncing his body he acquires the pre-eminent virtues of the Siddhas, by the possession [pg 168] of which he goes to the highest region of the universe, and becomes absolutely happy.  {#29:38}

39\. By renouncing company he obtains singleness; being single and concentrating his mind, he avoids disputes, quarrels, passions, and censoriousness, and he acquires a high degree of control, of Saṃvara, and of carefulness [^444].  {#29:39}

40\. By renouncing all food he prevents his being born again many hundreds of times.  {#29:40}

41\. By perfect renunciation [^445] he enters the final (fourth stage of pure meditation), whence there is no return; a monk who is in that state, destroys the four remnants of Karman which even a Kēvalin possesses, viz. vēdanīya, āyushka, nāman, and gōtra [^446]; and then he will put an end to all misery.  {#29:41}

42\. By conforming to the standard of monks [^447] he obtains ease, thereby he will be careful, wear openly the excellent badges of the order, be of perfect righteousness, possess firmness and the Samitis, inspire all beings with confidence, mind but few things [^448], subdue his senses, and practise, in a high degree, the Samitis and austerities.  {#29:42}

43\. By doing service he acquires the Karman [pg 169] which brings about for him the nāman and gōtra of a Tīrthakara.  {#29:43}

By fulfilling all virtues he secures that he will not be born again; thereby he will become exempt from pains of the body and mind.  {#29:44}

45\. By freedom from passion he cuts off the ties of attachment and desire; thereby he becomes indifferent to all agreeable and disagreeable sounds, touches, colours, and smells.  {#29:45}

46\. By patience he overcomes troubles.  {#29:46}

47\. By freedom from greed he obtains voluntary poverty, whereby he will become inaccessible to desire for property.  {#29:47}

48\. By simplicity he will become upright in actions, thoughts, and speech, and he will become veracious; thereby he will truly practise the Law.  {#29:48}

49\. By humility he will acquire freedom from self-conceit; thereby he will become of a kind and meek disposition, and avoid the eight kinds of pride.  {#29:49}

50\. By sincerity of mind he obtains purity of mind, which will cause him to exert himself for the fulfilment of the Law which the Jinas have proclaimed; and he will practise the Law in the next world too.  {#29:50}

51\. By sincerity in religious practice he obtains proficiency in it; being proficient in it he will act up to his words.  {#29:51}

52\. By sincerity of acting he will become pure in his actions.  {#29:52}

53\. By watchfulness [^449] of the mind he concentrates his thoughts; thereby he truly practises control.  {#29:53}

[pg 170]

54\. By watchfulness of speech he keeps free from prevarication; thereby he enables his mind to act properly.  {#29:54}

55\. By watchfulness of the body he obtains Saṃvara [^450]; thereby he prevents sinful Āsravas.  {#29:55}

56\. By discipline of the mind he obtains concentration of his thoughts; thereby he obtains development of knowledge, which produces righteousness and annihilates wrong belief.  {#29:56}

57\. By discipline of the speech he obtains development of faith, whereby he acquires facility of becoming enlightened, and destroys preventing causes.  {#29:57}

58\. By discipline of the body he obtains development of conduct, which causes him to conduct himself according to the regulation; thereby he destroys the four remnants of Karman which even a Kēvalin possesses [^451]; after that he obtains perfection, enlightenment, deliverance, and final beatitude, and he puts an end to all misery.  {#29:58}

59\. By possession of knowledge he acquires an understanding of words and their meaning; thereby he will not perish in the forest of the fourfold Saṃsāra; as a needle with its thread will not be lost, thus the soul possessing the sacred lore [^452] will not be lost in the Saṃsāra; he performs all prescribed actions relating to knowledge, discipline, austerities, and conduct, and well versed in his [pg 171] own and in heterodox creeds he will become invincible.  {#29:59}

60\. By possession of faith he annihilates wrong belief which is the cause of worldly existence, and he will not lose his inner light; but he endues his Self with the highest knowledge and faith, and purifies it [^453].  {#29:60}

61\. By possession of conduct he obtains a stability like that of the king of mountains [^454] (viz. Mēru), whereby a houseless monk destroys the four remnants of Karman which even a Kēvalin possesses; after that he obtains perfection, enlightenment, deliverance, and final beatitude, and puts an end to all misery.  {#29:61}

62\. By subduing the organ of hearing he overcomes his delight with or aversion to all pleasant or unpleasant sounds, he acquires no Karman produced thereby, and destroys the Karman he had acquired before.  {#29:62}

63-66. (All this applies also to his) subduing the organs of sight, of smelling, of tasting, and of touch (with regard to) pleasant colours, smells, tastes, and touches.  {#29:63}

67\. By conquering anger he obtains patience; he acquires no Karman productive of anger [^455], and destroys the Karman he had acquired before.  {#29:67}

68\. By conquering pride he obtains simplicity, &c. (as in 67, substituting pride for anger).  {#29:68}

69\. By conquering deceit he obtains humility, &c. (as in 67, substituting deceit for anger).  {#29:69}

[pg 172]

70\. By conquering greed he obtains content, &c. (as in 67, substituting greed for anger).  {#29:70}

71\. By conquering love, hate, and wrong belief he exerts himself for right knowledge, faith, and conduct, then he will cut off the fetters of the eightfold Karman; he will first destroy the twenty-eight kinds [^456] of Karman, which are productive of delusion; (then) the five kinds of obstruction to right knowledge [^457], the nine kinds of obstruction to right faith [^458], and the five kinds of obstacles (called Antarāya): the last three remnants of Karman he destroys simultaneously; afterwards he obtains absolute knowledge and faith, which is supreme, full, complete, unchecked, clear, faultless, and giving light (or penetrating) the whole universe; and while he still acts [^459], he acquires but such Karman as is inseparable from religious acts [^460]; the pleasant feelings (produced by it) last but two moments: in the first moment it is acquired, in the second it is experienced, and in the third it is destroyed; this Karman is produced, comes into contact (with the soul), takes rise, is experienced, and is destroyed; for all time to come he is exempt from Karman.  {#29:71}

72\. Then [^461] when his life is spent up to less than [pg 173] half a muhūrta, he discontinues to act, and enters upon the (third degree of) pure meditation [^462], from which there is no relapse (to lower degrees), and which requires most subtile functions only (of his organs); he first stops the functions of his mind, then the functions of speech, then those of the body, at last he ceases to breathe. During the time required for pronouncing five short syllables, he is engaged in the final pure meditation, in which all functions (of his organs) have ceased, and he simultaneously annihilates the four remnants of Karman, viz. vēdanīya, āyushka, nāman, and gōtra [^463].  {#29:72}

73\. Then having, by all methods, got rid of his audārika, kārmaṇa (and taijasa) bodies, the soul takes the form of a straight line, goes in one moment, without touching anything and taking up no space, (upwards to the highest Akāśa), and there develops into its natural form, obtains perfection, enlightenment, deliverance, and final beatitude, and puts an end to all misery.  {#29:73}

This indeed is the subject of the lecture called exertion in righteousness, which the Venerable Ascetic Mahāvīra has told, declared, explained, demonstrated.  {#29:74}

Thus I say.

[^421]: Here we have no less than ten verbs, many of which are synonyms, with probably no well-defined difference in their meaning. This heaping of synonymous words is a peculiarity of the archaic style. The commentators always labour hard to assign to each word an appropriate meaning, but by sometimes offering different sets of explanations they show that their ingenuity of interpretation was not backed by tradition.

[^422]: Or aversion to the Circle of Births.

[^423]: In this way all paragraphs up to § 72 open with a question of always the same form. I drop the question in the sequel.

[^424]: Atyāśātana.

[^425]: Nidāna, cf. p. 60, n. [^173].

[^426]: This is the meaning of the words itthīvēya napuṃsagavēyaṃ [pg 163] = strīvēda, napuṃsakavēda, as explained by the commentators on XXXII, 102.

[^427]: Karaṇaguṇaśrēdhīm pratipadyatē. It is difficult to render this phrase adequately; the meaning is that by successively destroying moral impurities one arrives at higher and higher virtues.

[^428]: Yōga, i.e. the cause of the production of Karman.

[^429]: Ghāti, compare Bhandarkar, Report, p. 93, note *.

[^430]: See Twenty-fourth Lecture, p. 129 ff.

[^431]: Expiatory rites, ālōcanā, &c.

[^432]: Antakriyā, explained by mukti.

[^433]: The Kalpas and the Vimānas are the heavens of the Vaimānika gods, see below, p. 226.

[^434]: By road is meant the means of acquiring right knowledge, and by the reward of the road, right knowledge. The reward of good conduct is mukti.

[^435]: Savvapāṇabhūyajīvasattā. The prāṇas possess from two to four organs of sense, the jīvas five, the bhūtas are plants, and the sattvas are all remaining beings.

[^436]: According to the commentaries, by Tīrtha are meant the Gaṇadharas.

[^437]: Concerning the eight kinds of Karman, see XXXIII, 2 and 3, p. 192. Āyushka is that Karman which determines the length of time which one is to live. A somewhat different explanation of this Karman is given by Bhandarkar, loc. cit., p. 97, note.

[^438]: The passage in question is an addition in some MSS., as the commentators tell us. The meaning seems to be that the Karman which was attached to many parts of the soul is restricted to fewer places by the influence of the purity superinduced on the soul by pondering.

[^439]: Vyavadāna is the cutting off of the Karman and the subsequent purity of the soul.

[^440]: Sambhōga = ēkamaṇḍalyām āhārakaraṇam.

[^441]: Ālambanā, glānatādi.

[^442]: Duccaṃ suhase.gjaṃ uvasaṃpajjittāṇaṃ viharai.

[^443]: Except such as are obligatory, e.g. his broom, the mukhavastrikā, &c.

[^444]: Samāhiē = samāhita or samādhimān.

[^445]: Sadbhāva pratyākhyāna. The Dīpikā gives the following explanation: he makes the renunciation in such a way that he need not make it a second time.

[^446]: Vēdanīya is that Karman which produces effects that must be experienced, as pleasure or pain; āyushka is the Karman that determines the length of life; nāman and gōtra cause him to be born as such or such an individual in this or that family; see Thirty-third Lecture, verses 2 and 3, p. 192 f.

[^447]: Explained: sthavirakalpasādhuvēshadhāritvam.

[^448]: Appaḍilēha = alpapratyupēksha; he has to inspect few things, because he uses only few.

[^449]: Gupti.

[^450]: For Saṃvara and Āsrava, see above, p. 55, note [^197].

[^451]: See above, § 41.

[^452]: Here is a pun on the word sutta = sūtra, which means thread and Sūtra, sacred lore, or knowledge acquired by the study of the Sūtras.

[^453]: I.e. makes it contain nothing foreign to its own nature.

[^454]: Sēlēsī = śailēśī; śailēśa is Mēru, and its avasthā, or condition, is śailēśī.

[^455]: Or, perhaps, which results in experiencing anger.

[^456]: There are sixteen kashāyas, nine nō-kashāyas, and three mōhanīyas.

[^457]: These are the obstacles to the five kinds of knowledge: mati, śruta, avadhi, manaḥparyāya, kēvala.

[^458]: They are: the obstacles to cakshurdarśana, to acakshurdarśana, to avadhidarśana, and to kēvaladarśana, and five kinds of sleep (nidrā). Concerning Antarāya, see p. 193.

[^459]: Sayōgin, i.e. while he has not yet reached the fourteenth guṇasthāna, the state of a Kēvalin.

[^460]: Airyapathika.

[^461]: I.e. when he has become a Kēvalin, as described in the preceding paragraph.

[^462]: Sukladhyāna.

[^463]: See note on § 41.

[pg 174]

## THIRTIETH LECTURE. THE ROAD OF PENANCE.

Now hear with concentrated mind, how a monk destroys by austerities the bad Karman which he had acquired by love and hatred.  {#30:1}

By abstaining 1. from destroying life; 2. from lying; 3. from taking anything which is not given; 4. from all sexual indulgence; 5. from having any property; and 6. from eating at night, the soul becomes free from Āsravas [^464].  {#30:2}

By possessing the five Samitis and the three Guptis, by freedom from passions, by subduing the senses, by vanquishing conceit [^465], and by avoiding delusions, the soul becomes free from Āsravas.  {#30:3}

Hear attentively how a monk destroys (the Karman) acquired by love and hatred in the absence of the above-mentioned (virtues).  {#30:4}

As a large tank, when its supply of water has been stopped, gradually dries up by the consumption of the water and by evaporation, so the Karman of a monk, which he acquired in millions [^466] of births, is annihilated by austerities, if there is no influx of bad Karman.  {#30:5}

Austerities are of two kinds: external and internal; [pg 175] external austerities are of six kinds, and internal are of six kinds [^467].  {#30:7}

External austerities are: {#30:8}

1\. anaśana, fasting; 2. avamōdarikā, abstinence [^468]; 3\. bhikshācaryā, collecting alms; 4. rasaparityāga, abstention from dainty food; 5. kāyaklēśa, mortification of the flesh; 6. saṃlīnatā, taking care of one's limbs [^469]. 

1\. Fasting is of two kinds: *a*. itvara, temporary, and *b*. maraṇakāla, fasting which precedes, and ends with death. Temporary fasting is either such in which a desire (for food) is present, or such in which no such desire exists.  {#30:9}

*a*. The temporary fasting is briefly of six kinds: 1. in the form of a line [^470]; 2\. in the form of a square; 3. in the form of a cube; 4. of a sixth power; 5. of a twelfth power; 6. of any arrangement. Temporary fasting (can be practised) for different objects which one has in mind.  {#30:10}

[pg 176]

*b*. Fasting which is to precede death, is of two kinds with regard to the motions of the body: with change (of position) and without change.  {#30:12}

And again it is twofold: admitting of relief [^471], or not; one may either leave the place (which one has chosen to die in), or not leave it; in both cases one may not take any food.  {#30:13}

2\. Abstinence is briefly of five kinds: with regard to *a*. substance; *b*. place; *c*. time; ḍ. state of mind; *e*. development.  {#30:14}

*a*. He who takes less food than he usually does [^472], in the extreme case but one mouthful, performs abstinence with regard to substance.  {#30:15}

*b*. (Place means) a village, a scotfree town [^473], a capital, a camp of merchants [^474], a mine, a settlement of a wild tribe [^475], a place with an earth wall [^476], a poor town [^477], a town with a harbour [^478], a large town [^479], an isolated town [^480], and an open town [^481].  {#30:16}

[pg 177]

In a hermitage, a vihāra [^482], a halting-place for procession [^483], a resting-place for travellers [^484], a station of herdsmen, a camp on high ground, a caravan's camp, a fortified place of refuge.  {#30:17}

In gardens, on roads, in houses--all this is meant by place. In these and similar places he may (wander about). In this way he performs abstinence with regard to place.  {#30:18}

1\. pēṭā, 2\. ardhapēṭā, 3\. gōmūtrikā, 4. pataṅgavīthikā, 5. śambūkāvartta, 6. āyataṃ-gatvā-pratyāgata [^485].  {#30:19}

*c*. Abstinence with reference to time (is observed by him) who goes about in that time of the four Paurushīs of the day (which he selects for that purpose).  {#30:20}

Or if he collects alms in a part of the third Paurushī, or in its last quarter, then he observes abstinence with reference to time.  {#30:21}

ḍ. Abstinence with reference to state of mind (is observed by him) who accepts alms from a woman or man, from an adorned or unadorned person, from one of any age or dress, of any temper or colour: if that person does not change his disposition or condition [^486].  {#30:22}

[pg 178]

*e*. A monk who observes abstinence according to the particulars which have been enumerated with regard to substance, place, time, and state of mind, observes abstinence with regard to development [^487] too.  {#30:24}

3\. With regard to collecting alms there are the eight principal ways [^488] how to collect them; the seven ēshaṇās (or modes of begging) and other self-imposed restrictions.  {#30:25}

4\. Abstention from dainty food means abstention from such highly nourishing [^489] food and drink as milk, curds, ghee, &c.  {#30:26}

5\. Mortification of the flesh consists in the different postures as Virāsana, &c., which benefit the soul, and which are difficult to perform.  {#30:27}

6\. Using unfrequented lodgings and beds consists in living and sleeping in separate and [pg 179] unfrequented places where there are neither women nor cattle.  {#30:28}

Thus external austerities have been briefly explained; I shall now explain internal austerities in due order.  {#30:29}

Internal austerities are: {#30:30}

1\. prāyaścitta, expiation of sins;

2\. viṇaya, politeness;

3\. vaiyāvṛtya, serving the Guru;

4\. svādhyāya, study;

5\. dhyāna, meditation;

6\. vyutsarga [^490], abandoning of the body. 

1\. Expiation of sins is tenfold, what must be confessed [^491], &c.; this is to be strictly observed by a monk; this is called expiation of sins.  {#30:31}

2\. Politeness consists in rising (from one's seat), folding of the hands, offering of a seat, loving the Guru, and cordial obedience.  {#30:32}

3\. There are ten [^492] kinds of service, as serving the Ācārya, &c. [^493]; doing service consists in giving one's assistance as well as one is able.  {#30:33}

4\. Study is fivefold: 1. saying or learning one's lesson; 2. (questioning the teacher about it); 3. repetition; 4. pondering; 5. religious discourse.  {#30:34}

[pg 180]

5\. Abstaining to meditate on painful and sinful things [^494], one should, with a collected mind, engage in pure meditations on the Law; this the wise call meditation.  {#30:35}

6\. If a monk remains motionless when lying down, sitting, or standing upright, this is called abandoning of the body, which is the sixth kind (of internal austerities).  {#30:36}

If a sage truly performs these two kinds of austerities, he will soon be thoroughly released from the Circle of Births.  {#30:37}

Thus I say.

[^464]: Karmōpādānahētavas, that through which the soul becomes affected by Karman.

[^465]: Agārava = agaurava; but it is here explained, free from garva, cf. p. 98, note [^257].

[^466]: Literally krores, i.e. ten millions.

[^467]: Comp. Aupapātika Sūtra, ed. Leumann, p. 38 ff. The general division is the same, but the subdivision differs in many details.

[^468]: Gradual reduction of food, from a full meal of thirty-two morsels to one of one morsel.

[^469]: Angōpāṅgādikam saṃvṛtya pravartanam, Ṭīkā.

[^470]: The meaning of this singular statement is as follows. If four fasts of two, three, four, and five days are performed in this order, they form a line. If this set of fasts is four times repeated, each time beginning with a different number, we get sixteen fasts; they form a square, viz.:

    |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |
    | - | - | - | - | - | - | - |
    |   1   |   .   |   2   |   .   |   3   |   .   |   4   |
    |   2   |   .   |   3   |   .   |   4   |   .   |   1   |
    |   3   |   .   |   4   |   .   |   1   |   .   |   2   |
    |   4   |   .   |   1   |   .   |   2   |   .   |   3   |

    The next class contains 64 fasts, the fourth 4,096, the fifth 16,777,216 fasts. Fasts of the last class require 700,000 years at least, and must be assumed to be restricted to former Tīrthakaras, whose lives lasted enormous periods of time.

[^471]: Saparikarma = vaiyāvṛtyasahita. This leads to iṅginīmaraṇa and bhaktapratyakhyāna; the aparikarma to pādapōpagamana (i.e. prāyōpagamana); comp. part i, p. 72.

[^472]: Thirty-two mouthfuls is the usual quantity of food of men, twenty-eight that of women. A mouthful is of the size of an egg.

[^473]: Nagara, where no taxes (na kara) are levied, while villages pay eighteen taxes.

[^474]: Nigama, or a place where many merchants dwell.

[^475]: Pallī.

[^476]: Khēṭa.

[^477]: Karvaṭa. According to the dictionary, it means 'market-town;' but the commentators render it by kunagara, or say that it is karvaṭajanāvāsa, the dwelling-place of the Karvaṭa people.

[^478]: Drōṇamukha, a town to which there is access by water and land, like Bhṛgukaccha or Tāmralipti.

[^479]: Paṭṭana.

[^480]: Maṭamba, a town which is more than three and a half yōjanas distant from the next village.

[^481]: Sambādha, prabhūtacāturvarṇyanivāsa.

[^482]: A dwelling-place of Bhikshus, or a dēvagṛha.

[^483]: Sannivēśa.

[^484]: Samāja.

[^485]: These are terms for different kinds of collecting alms; it is called pēṭā (box), when one begs successively at four houses forming the corners of an imaginary square; gōmūtrikā, when he takes the houses in a zigzag line; pataṅgavīthikā (cricket's walk), when he goes to houses at a great distance from one another; śambūkāvartta (the windings of a conch), when he goes in a spiral line, either toward the centre (abhyantara) or from the centre outward (bahis); āyataṃ-gatvā-pratyāgata, when he first goes straight on and then returns.

[^486]: I give the traditional explanation of the verses, as handed [pg 178] down in the commentaries. If we might set it aside, I should translate: abstinence with reference to disposition is observed by him who in collecting alms preserves the same disposition, whether he has to do with a woman or man, &c.

[^487]: For development (pajjava = paryāya) denotes any form or phase of existence which anything can assume. Therefore all particulars of place, e.g. are developments of Place. As all restrictions of place, &c., indirectly diminish the food obtainable by a monk, they also come under the head Abstinence.

[^488]: According to the commentator, these are the six kinds enumerated in verse 29. Sambūkāvartta is of two kinds, as explained in the note; the eighth kind is ṛjvī, or the common way of begging. These eight ways have reference to the houses in which they collect alms. The seven ēshaṇās refer to the quality or quantity of the food; their names are given in the Ṭīkā, partly in Prākṛt, partly in Sanskrit: 1. saṃsaṭṭhā; 2\. asaṃsaṭṭhā; 3\. uddhaḍā; 4\. alpalēpikā; 5. udgṛhītā; 6. pragṛhītā; 7. ujjhitadharmā. According to another passage: 1. is saṃspṛshṭa, 3. uddhrita, 5. avagṛhīta.

[^489]: Praṇīta, explained pushṭikara.

[^490]: Viōsagga, viussaga, viusagga. It is usually rendered vyutsarga, but the Sanskrit prototype is vyavasarga, as Leumann has pointed out, l.c., p. 152.

[^491]: Compare Aupapātika Sūtra, ed. Leumann, p. 40.

[^492]: Ibidem, p. 42.

[^493]: They are enumerated in the following Gāthā: āyariya-uvajjhāē thēra-tavassī-gilāṇa-sēhāṇa | sāhhmmiya-kula-gaṇa-saṅgha-saṃgayaṃ tam iha kāyavvaṃ. The ten persons or body of persons entitled to 'service' are: 1. ācārya; 2. upādhyāya; 3. sthavira; 4. tapasvin; 5. glāna; 6. śaiksha; 7. sādharmika; 8. kula; 9. gaṇa; 10. saṅgha.

[^494]: This is the ārtaraudradhyāna.

* * *

## THIRTY-FIRST LECTURE. MODE OF LIFE [^495].

I shall declare the mode of life that benefits the soul; by practising it many souls have crossed the ocean of Saṃsāra.  {#31:1}

One should desist from one thing, and practise another: desist from neglect of self-control, and practise self-control.  {#31:2}

Love and hatred are two evils which produce bad [pg 181] Karman; if a monk always avoids them, he will not stand within the circle (of transmigration).  {#31:3}

A monk who always avoids the thrice threefold hurtful, conceited, and delusive acts [^496], will not stand in the circle (of transmigration).  {#31:4}

A monk who well bears calamities produced by gods, animals, or men, will not stand, &c.  {#31:5}

A monk who always avoids the (four) different kinds of praises [^497], passions, expressions (of the emotions) [^498], and (of the four) meditations the two sinful ones, will not stand, &c.  {#31:6}

A monk who always exerts himself [^499] with regard to the (five) vows, the (five) objects of sense, the (five) Samitis, and (five) actions [^500], will not stand, &c.  {#31:7}

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the six lēśyās [^501], the six kinds of bodies, and the six (regular functions as) eating [^502], will not stand, &c.  {#31:8}

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the (seven) rules of accepting alms [^503], and the seven causes of danger (to other men) will not stand, &c.  {#31:9}

[pg 182]

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the (eight) objects of pride [^504], to that which protects his chastity [^505], and to the tenfold Law of the monks [^506].  {#31:10}

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the (eleven) duties of the upāsakas, and the (twelve) duties of the bhikshus [^507], will not stand, &c.  {#31:11}

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the (thirteen) actions (productive of Karman), to the various (fourteen) kinds of living beings, and the (fifteen) places of punishment of the wicked [^508], will not  stand, &c.  {#31:12}

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the sixteen Gāthās [^509], and to the (seventeen kinds of) neglect of self-control, will not stand, &c.  {#31:13}

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the (eighteen kinds of) continence, to the (nineteen) jñātādhyayanas [^510], and the (twenty) cases for not concentrating one's thoughts, will not, &c.  {#31:14}

[pg 183]

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the twenty-one forbidden [^511] actions, and the twenty-two troubles [^512], will not stand, &c.  {#31:15}

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the twenty-three (lectures of the) Sūtrakṛtāṅga, and to the gods whose number exceeds by an unit [^513] (the number of the lectures of the Sūtrakṛtāṅga), will not stand, &c. (t 6) {#31:17}

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the twenty-five clauses [^514], and (to the recitation of the twenty-six) chapters of the Daśās, &c. [^515], will not stand, &c. 

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the (twenty-seven) virtues of the laity, and the (twenty-eight lectures of the) Prakalpa [^516], will not stand, &c. 08) {#31:19}

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the (twenty-nine) causes of wrong knowledge, and the (thirty) causes of delusion, will not stand, &c. 

A monk who always exerts himself with regard to the (thirty-one) qualities of Siddhas, &c., the (thirty-two)

[pg 184]

Yogas [^517], and thirty-three Āśātanās [^518], will not stand, &c.  {#31:20}

A clever monk who always exerts himself with regard to the above-mentioned points, will soon be thoroughly released from the Circle of Births  {#31:21}

Thus I say.

[^495]: This lecture offers many difficulties to the translator, as it contains scarcely more than a dry list of articles of the Jaina faith. To fully understand or interpret it would require an accurate knowledge of the complete religious system of the Jainas, to which we can lay no claim at present. The order in which the articles are given follows the number of their subdivisions. In some cases the number is not given in the Sūtra, but is supplied by the commentary.

[^496]: Compare XIX, 91, and XXX, 3. Hurtful acts (daṇḍa) are threefold, as referring to thoughts, words, and acts; conceited acts (gārava), as pride of riches, of taste (rasa), and of pleasure or fashion (sātā); delusive acts (śalya), as māyā, nidāna, and mithyādarśana.

[^497]: Vikatthā.

[^498]: Saṃjñā.

[^499]: Yatatē 'exerts himself;' supply 'to avoid, to know, or to do,' as the case may require.

[^500]: Kriyā; they are: 1. kāyikī; 2. adhikaraṇikī; 3. prādvēshikī; 4. paritāpanikī, and 5. prāṇātipātikī.

[^501]: On the lēśyās see Thirty-fourth Lecture, p. 196 ff.

[^502]: From the commentaries I learn two more of these six kāraṇas: vēdana and vaiyāvṛtya. I cannot say which are the remaining three.

[^503]: They are enumerated in note  [^497] on XXX, 25, p. 178.

[^504]: Viz. caste, family, beauty, &c.; see Sūtrakṛt. II, 2, 17.

[^505]: Brahmagupti. This is of nine kinds.

[^506]: Bhikshudharma. It consists of Nos. 46-49, 26, 27, of Lecture XXIX, truth, purity, poverty, and chastity.

[^507]: The details given in the commentary (Dēvēndra) partly differ from the description of the twelve duties of Śrāvakas, and the ten duties of Bhikshus given by Bhandarkar from the Kārttikēyānuprēkshā, see his Report, p. 114 ff.

[^508]: Paramādhārmika. My translation is based on the enumeration of fifteen words, among which the names of some well-known hells occur.

[^509]: The sixteen lectures of the first part of the Sūtrakṛtāṅga, the last of which is called Gāthā, are meant by the sixteen Gāthās. The whole book contains twenty-three lectures as stated in verse 16.

[^510]: The first śrutaskandha of the Jñātādharmakathā, which contains nineteen adhyayanas, is intended by jñātādhyayana.

[^511]: Śabala, because they 'variegate' the conduct. The actions meant are sitting on an unwiped seat, &c.

[^512]: Parīsaha, see above, p. 9 ff.

[^513]: Rūpa. The twenty-four gods are: ten Bhavanapatis, eight Vyantaras, five Jyōtishkas, one Vaimānika; or the 24 prophets.

[^514]: Bhāvanā, the subdivisions of the five great vows, see part i, p. 189 ff.

[^515]: The Daśāśrutaskandha, Bṛhat Kalpa, and Vyavahāra Sūtras are meant, which together contain twenty-six uddēśas.

[^516]: I.e. the Ācārāṅga Sūtra; it now contains but twenty-four lectures, but is said to have originally contained four more, see part i, introduction, p. xlix f. These four lectures were: Mahāparinnā, Ugghāya, Aṇugghāya, Ārōvaṇā.

[^517]: The pure operations of mind, speech, and body.

[^518]: As far as I can make out from the enumeration in the commentary, they are articles regulating the intercourse between monks, especially pupils and teacher.

* * *

## THIRTY-SECOND LECTURE. THE CAUSES OF CARELESSNESS.

With attentive mind hear me explain for your benefit the deliverance from the beginningless time, together with its causes [^519], and from all misery: a truly wholesome subject.  {#32:1}

By the teaching of true [^520] knowledge, by the avoidance of ignorance and delusion, and by the destruction of love and hatred, one arrives at final deliverance which is nothing but bliss.  {#32:2}

This is the road to it: to serve the Gurus and the old (teachers), to avoid throughout foolish people, to [pg 185] apply oneself earnestly to study, and to ponder zealously on the meaning of the Sūtras.  {#32:3}

A Śramaṇa engaged in austerities, who longs for righteousness [^521], should eat the proper quantity of allowed food, should select a companion of right understanding, and should live in a place suited to seclusion.  {#32:4}

If he does not meet with a clever companion who surpasses or equals him in virtue, he should live by himself, abstaining from sins and not devoted to pleasures.  {#32:5}

As the crane [^522] is produced from an egg, and the egg is produced from a crane, so they call desire [^523] the origin of delusion, and delusion the origin of desire.  {#32:6}

Love and hatred are caused by Karman, and they say that Karman has its origin in delusion; Karman is the root of birth and death, and birth and death they call misery.  {#32:7}

Misery ceases on the absence of delusion, delusion ceases on the absence of desire, desire ceases on the absence of greed, greed ceases on the absence of property.  {#32:8}

I shall explain in due order the means which must be adopted by him who wants to thoroughly uproot love, hatred, and delusion.  {#32:9}

Pleasant food [^524] should not be enjoyed with preference, for it generally makes men over-strong [^525]; and desires rush upon the strong, like birds upon a tree with sweet fruits.  {#32:10}

[pg 186]

As in a forest, full of fuel, a fire fanned by the wind cannot be extinguished, so the fire (as it were) of the senses of him who eats as he lists; it does not benefit any chaste man.  {#32:11}

The mind of those who always live in unfrequented lodgings, who eat low food, and who subdue their senses, will not be attacked by the foe, Love, who is vanquished as disease is by medicine.  {#32:12}

As it is not safe for mice to live near the dwelling of a cat, so a chaste (monk) cannot stay in a house inhabited by women.  {#32:13}

A Śramaṇa, engaged in penance, should not allow himself to watch the shape, beauty, coquetry, laughter, prattle, gestures, and glances of women, nor retain a recollection of them in his mind.  {#32:14}

Not to look at, nor to long for, not to think of, nor to praise, womankind: this is becoming the meditation of the noble ones, and it is always wholesome to those who delight in chastity.  {#32:15}

Though those who possess the three Guptis, cannot be disturbed even by well-adorned goddesses, still it is recommended to monks to live by themselves, as this is wholesome in every way.  {#32:16}

To a man who longs for liberation, who is afraid of the Saṃsāra, and lives according to the Law, nothing in the world offers so many difficulties [^526] as women who delight the mind of the ignorant.  {#32:17}

To those who have overcome the attachment (to women), all others will offer no difficulties [^527]; even as to those who have crossed the great ocean, no river, though big like the Ganges, (will offer any difficulty).  {#32:18}

[pg 187]

From desire of pleasure arises the misery of the whole world, the gods included; whatever misery of body and mind there is, the dispassionate will put an end to it.  {#32:19}

As the fruit of the Kimpāka [^528] is beautiful in taste and colour, when eaten; but destroys the life when digested, (being) poison; similar in their effect are pleasures.  {#32:20}

A Śramaṇa, engaged in austerities, who longs for righteousness [^529], should not fix his thoughts on the pleasant objects of the senses, nor turn his mind from them, if they be unpleasant.  {#32:21}

'Colour' attracts the eye; it is the pleasant cause of Love, but the unpleasant cause of Hatred [^530]; he who is indifferent to them (viz. colours), is called dispassionate.  {#32:22}

The eye perceives 'colour,' and 'colour' attracts the eye; the cause of Love is pleasant, and the cause of Hatred is unpleasant.  {#32:23}

He who is passionately fond of 'colours,' will come to untimely ruin; just as an impassioned moth which is attracted by the light rushes into death.  {#32:24}

He who passionately hates (a colour), will at the same moment suffer pain. It is the fault of an undisciplined man that he is annoyed (by a colour); it is not the 'colour' itself that annoys him.  {#32:25}

[pg 188]

He who is very fond of a lovely 'colour,' hates all others; hence a fool will suffer misery, but a dispassionate sage is not affected by it.  {#32:26}

He who has a passion for 'colours [^531],' will kill many movable and immovable beings; a passionate fool, intent on his personal interest, pains and torments those beings in many ways.  {#32:27}

How can a man who passionately desires 'colours [^532],' be happy while he gets, keeps, uses, loses, and misses (those things). Even when he enjoys them, he is never satisfied.  {#32:28}

When he is not satisfied with those 'colours,' and his craving for them grows stronger and stronger, he will become discontented, and unhappy by dint of his discontent; misled by greed he will take another's property.  {#32:29}

When he is overcome by violent desire, takes another's property, and is not satisfied with those 'colours' and their possession, then his deceit and falsehood increase on account of his greed; yet he will not get rid of his misery.  {#32:30}

After and before he has lied [^533], and when he is on the point of lying, he feels infinitely unhappy. Thus when he takes another's property, and is (after all) not satisfied by the 'colours' (he has [pg 189] obtained), he becomes unhappy, and nobody will protect him [^534].  {#32:31}

How, then, can a man who is devoted to 'colours,' ever derive any happiness from anything? He suffers pain at the time of their enjoyment to procure which he had suffered misery.  {#32:32}

In the same way he who hates 'colours,' incurs a long succession of pains; when his mind is filled with hatred, he accumulates Karman which in the end again produces misery.  {#32:33}

But a man who is indifferent to 'colours,' is free from sorrows; though still in the Saṃsāra, he is not affected by that long succession of pains, just as the leaf of the Lotus (is not moistened) by water.  {#32:34}

\[The whole set of verses 22-34 is, with few alterations, five times repeated in the original in order to apply to the other organs of sense. {#32:50}

Verses 35-47 treat of sounds; 'sound' is to be substituted for 'colour,' 'ear' for 'eye.'

The last line of verse 37, which corresponds to verse 24, runs thus:

As an impassioned deer allured (by a song) rushes into death, without being satisfied with the sound.

In the same way verses 48-60 apply to smells'; substitute 'smell' and 'organ of smell.'

Verses 61-73 apply to tastes; substitute 'tastes' and 'tongue.'

Verses 74-86 apply to touches; substitute touches' and 'body.'

Verses 87-99 apply to feelings; substitute 'feelings' and 'mind.' [pg 190] The lines corresponding to the comparison in verse 24, run as follows:

Just as an impassioned snake which is allured by the smell of a drug, when it comes out of its hole. 

Just as an impassioned fish which is eager to swallow the bait, has its body transfixed by a hook.  {#32:63}

Just as an impassioned buffalo who dives in cold water, is taken hold of by a crocodile and dies.  {#32:76}

Just as an impassioned elephant who is inflamed by carnal desires, is turned from his way by a female elephant (and is captured and at last killed in battle).  {#32:89}

Thus the objects of the senses and of the mind cause pain to passionate men, but they never in the least cause any pain to the dispassionate.  {#32:100}

Pleasant things (by themselves) do not cause indifference nor emotions (as anger, &c.); but by either hating or loving them, a man undergoes such a change through delusion.  {#32:101}

Anger, pride, deceit, greed; disgust, aversion to self-control and delight in sensual things [^535]; mirth, fear, sorrow, carnal desire for women, men, or both; all these manifold passions arise in him who is attached to pleasures; and so do other emotions produced by those (before mentioned) arise in him who is to be pitied, who (ought to be) ashamed of himself, and who is hateful.  {#32:102}

[pg 191]

A monk should not desire a companion, not (even) one who is able to perform his religious duties; nor, if he regrets having taken the vows, (should he desire for) a worldly reward of his austerities [^536]. Such emotions of an infinite variety arise in one who is the slave of his senses.  {#32:104}

Desiring happiness and being submerged in the ocean of delusion, he forms many plans for warding off misery; and for their sake an impassioned man exerts himself.  {#32:105}

But all kinds of objects of the senses, sounds, &c., will cause to the indifferent neither a pleasant nor an unpleasant feeling.  {#32:106}

He who endeavours to recognise the vanity of all desires [^537], will arrive at perfect indifference. When he ceases to desire the objects (of the senses), his desire for pleasures will become extinct.  {#32:107}

The dispassionate man who has performed all duties will quickly remove the obstructions to right knowledge and to right faith, and whatever Karman produces obstruction (to righteousness).  {#32:108}

Then he knows and sees all things, he is free from delusion and hindrances, his Āsravas have gone, [pg 192] and he is proficient in meditation and concentration of thoughts, and being pure he will arrive at beatitude when his life is spent.  {#32:109}

He will get rid of all misery which always afflicts mankind; recovered from the long illness, as it were, and glorious, he becomes infinitely happy, and obtains the (final) aim.  {#32:110}

We have taught the way how to become exempt from all misery which arises since time without beginning; those beings who follow it will in their time become infinitely happy.  {#32:111}

Thus I say.

[^519]: By beginningless time the Saṃsāra is meant; its causes are the kashāyas or cardinal passions, and avirati.

[^520]: Saccassa = satyasya. This is a various reading; the received text has savvassa. The commentators give the following explanation: by the property of knowledge to make everything known--this indicates that knowledge is the cause of mōksha.

[^521]: Samādhi; the Dīpikā explains it by jñānadarśanacāritralābha.

[^522]: Balāka.

[^523]: Tṛshṇā.

[^524]: Rasā.

[^525]: Dṛptikara.

[^526]: Duttara.

[^527]: Suuttara.

[^528]: Trichosanthes Palmata, or Cucumis Colocynthus.

[^529]: Compare verse 4.

[^530]: Love and Hatred must of course be understood in their widest meaning. The same remark applies to the term 'colour,' which according to Hindu terminology denotes everything that is perceived by the eye. The first three sentences are, in the original, dependent on verbs as vadanti, āhus. I have, here and elsewhere, dropped them in the translation.

[^531]: Rūvāṇugāsāṇuga = rūpa-anuga-āśā-anuga. This division of the compound looks artificial; I should prefer to divide rūva-aṇugāsa-aṇuga = rūpa-anukarsha-anuga; literally, possessed of attraction by colours.

[^532]: Rūvaṇuvāēṇa pariggahēṇa. Parigraha is explained as the desire to possess them.

[^533]: Instead of 'lying,' we can also adopt the rendering 'stealing,' as the word in the original mōsa may stand either for mṛshā, or for mōsha.

[^534]: Aṇissa = aniśra. Niśrā does not occur in common Sanskrit; it is rendered avashṭambha by the commentators.

[^535]: Arati and rati. Compare note on XXI, 21, where I have adopted another translation suited to the context. The first four numbers contain the cardinal passions; the rest the emotions which are called nō-kashāya.

[^536]: My translation follows the interpretation of the commentators. The original runs thus: Kappaṃ na icchijja sahāyalicchū pacchāṇutāvēṇa tavappabhāvaṃ. The meaning they have made out is very unsatisfactory. There is a remarkable various reading in MS. C not noticed by the scholiasts: sahāyalacchiṃ = svabhāvalakshmīṃ. If this was the original reading, the meaning of the line, in which however I must leave the word kappam untranslated, would come to this: a monk who regrets having taken the vows should not desire personal power as the reward for his penance. Kalpa, according to the commentators, is one who is able to perform his religious duties; a kalpa is contrasted with a śishya, novice.

[^537]: Saṃkalpavikalpanāsu upasthitasya.

* * *

## THIRTY-THIRD LECTURE. THE NATURE OF KARMAN.

I shall now in due order explain the eight kinds of Karman, bound by which the soul turns round and round in the Circle of Births.  {#33:1}

The eight kinds of Karman are briefly the following: {#33:2}

1\. Jñānāvaraṇīya (which acts as an obstruction to right knowledge);

2\. Darśanāvaraṇīya (which acts as an obstruction to right faith);

3\. Vēdanīya (which leads to experiencing pain or pleasure);

4\. Mōhanīya (which leads to delusion);

5\. Āyuḥkarman (which determines the length of life); [pg 193] 6\. Nāman (which determines the name or individuality of the embodied soul);

7\. Gōtra (which determines his Gōtra);

8\. Antarāya (which prevents one's entrance on the path that leads to eternal bliss [^538]). 

1\. Obstruction of knowledge is fivefold (viz. obstruction to): {#33:4}

*a*. Śruta, knowledge derived from the sacred books;

*b*. Ābhinibōdhika, perception;

*c*. Avadhijñāna, supernatural knowledge;

ḍ. Manaḥparyāya, knowledge of the thoughts of other people;

*e*. Kēvala, the highest, unlimited knowledge. 

2\. The nine kinds of obstruction to right faith are: 1. sleep; 2. activity; 3. very deep sleep; 4. a high degree of activity [^539]; 5\. a state of deep-rooted greed; 6-9 refer to faith in the objects of the first three and the last kinds of knowledge.  {#33:5}

3\. Vēdanīya is twofold, pleasure and pain; there are many subdivisions of pleasure and so there are of pain also.  {#33:7}

4\. Mōhanīya is twofold as referring to faith and to conduct; the first is threefold, the second twofold.  {#33:8}

The three kinds of Mōhanīya referring to faith are: 1. right faith; 2. wrong faith; 3. faith partly right and partly wrong.  {#33:9}

[pg 194]

The two kinds of Mōhanīya referring to conduct are: 1. what is experienced in the form of the four cardinal passions; 2. what is experienced in the form of feelings different from them.  {#33:10}

The first kind of this Karman is sixteenfold, the second sevenfold or ninefold [^540].  {#33:11}

5\. Āyushka is fourfold as referring to 1. denizens of hell; 2. brute creation; 3. men; 4. gods.  {#33:12}

6\. Nāman is twofold, good and bad; there are many subdivisions of the good variety, and so there are of the bad one also [^541].  {#33:13}

7\. Gōtra is twofold, high and low; the first is eightfold, and so is the second also.  {#33:14}

8\. Antarāya is fivefold as preventing: 1. gifts; 2. profit; 3. momentary enjoyment; 4. continuous enjoyment [^542]; and 5. power.  {#33:15}

Thus the division of Karman and the subdivisions have been told. {#33:16}

Now hear their number of atoms [^543], place, time, and development. 

[pg 195]

The number of atoms of every Karman is infinite; it is (infinitely) greater than (the number) of fettered [^544] souls, but less than that of the perfected ones.  {#33:17}

The Karman in the six directions of space [^545] binds all souls, and it binds the whole soul in all its parts in every possible way.  {#33:18}

The longest duration (of Karman) is thirty Krores of Krores of Sāgarōpamās [^546], and the shortest a part of a muhūrta.  {#33:19}

This holds good with both Āvaraṇīyas, with Vēdanīya and Antarāya.  {#33:20}

The longest duration of Mōhanīya is seventy Krores of Krores of Sāgarōpamās, and the shortest a part of a muhūrta.  {#33:21}

The longest duration of Āyushka is thirty-three Krores of Krores of Sāgarōpamās, and the shortest a part of a muhūrta.  {#33:22}

The longest duration of Nāman and Gōtra is twenty Krores of Krores of Sāgarōpamās, and the shortest eight muhūrtas.  {#33:23}

The number of perfected souls is infinite, and that [pg 196] of the subdivisions of Karman [^547] is also (infinite); the number of atoms in all these (subdivisions) exceeds (the number) of all souls.  {#33:24}

Therefore a wise man should know the different subdivisions of these Karmans, and should exert himself to prevent and to destroy them.  {#33:25}

Thus I say.

[^538]: Compare Bhandarkar, Report, p. 93, note *.

[^539]: Nos. 1-4 are nidrā, pracalā, nidrānidrā, pracalāpracalā; I render the etymological meaning of these words. According to the Dīpikā, however, they have a different meaning: nidrā means the state of agreeable waking; pracalā, the slumber of a standing or sitting person; nidrānidrā, deep sleep; pracalāpracalā, sleep of a person in motion. Nos. 6 and 7 are here called cakkhu and acakkhu, instead of ābhinibōdhika and śruta.

[^540]: The divisions of the second Karman are the feelings or emotions enumerated in the 102nd verse of the last lecture, from disgust onward. There are seven of them, if desire for women, men, or both, is reckoned as one item, but nine, if it is reckoned as three. The sixteen divisions of the Karman produced by the cardinal passions are arrived at by subdividing each of the four passions with reference to 1. anantānubandha; 2. pratyākhyāna; 3. apratyākhyāna; 4. saṃjvalana.

[^541]: In the Dīpikā 103 subdivisions are enumerated; they correspond to our genera.

[^542]: 3\. Bhōga, 4. upabhōga; bhōga is enjoyment of flowers, food, &c.; upabhōga, that of one's house, wife, &c. The Karman in question brings about an obstruction to the enjoyment, &c., though all other circumstances be favourable.

[^543]: The Karman is considered to consist, like other substances, of atoms, here called pradēśa point. The word I have translated [pg 195] number of atoms is paēsaggaṃ = pradēśāgram, which is rendered paramāṇuparimāṇa.

[^544]: Gaṇṭhiyasatta = granthigasattva.

[^545]: The six directions of space are the four cardinal points, zenith and nadir. The commentators quote scripture that ēkēndriyas, or beings with one organ of sense, are bound by Karman in three and more directions. The true meaning of this statement is beyond my grasp.--The Dīpikā explains how Karman acts on the soul. The soul absorbs all material particles of a suitable nature (especially the karmapudgalas) with which it comes into contact, i.e. all that are in the same space with the soul, and assimilates them in the form of jñānāvaranīya, &c., just as fire consumes everything within its reach, but nothing beyond it.

[^546]: I.e. 3,000,000,000,000,000 Sāgarōpamās.

[^547]: Anubhāga, explained karmarasaviśēsha.

* * *

## THIRTY-FOURTH LECTURE. ON LĒŚYĀ [^548].

I shall deliver in due order the Lecture on Lēśyā; hear the nature of the six Lēśyās (produced by) Karman.  {#34:1}

[pg 197]

Hear 1. the names, 2. colours, 3. tastes, 4. smells, 5. touches, 6. degrees, 7. character, 8. variety, 9. duration, 10. result, and 11. life of the Lēśyās.  {#34:2}

1\. They are named in the following order: black, blue, grey, red, yellow, and white.  {#34:3}

2\. The black Lēśyā has the colour of a rain-cloud, a buffalo's horn, (the fruit of) Rishṭaka [^549], or the eye of the wagtail.  {#34:4}

The blue Lēśyā has the colour of the blue Aśōka [^550], the tail of the Casha [^551], or of lapis lazuli.  {#34:5}

The grey Lēśyā has the colour of the flower of Atasī [^552], the feathers of the Kōkila, or the collar of pigeons.  {#34:6}

The red Lēśyā has the colour of vermilion, the rising sun, or the bill of a parrot.  {#34:7}

The yellow Lēśyā has the colour of orpiment, turmeric, or the flowers of Śaṇa [^553] and Asana [^554].  {#34:8}

The white Lēśyā has the colour of a conch-shell, the aṅka-stone [^555], Kunda-flowers [^556], flowing milk, silver, or a necklace of pearls.  {#34:9}

3\. The taste of the black Lēśyā is infinitely more bitter than that of Tumbaka [^557], (the fruit of the) Nimb-tree [^558], or of Rōhiṇī.  {#34:10}

[pg 198]

The taste of the blue Lēśyā is infinitely more pungent than Trikaṭuka [^559] and Hastipippalī.  {#34:11}

The taste of grey Lēśyā is infinitely sourer than that of unripe Mango and Kapittha [^560].  {#34:12}

The taste of red Lēśyā is infinitely more pleasant than that of ripe Mango and Kapittha.  {#34:13}

The taste of yellow Lēśyā is infinitely better than that of excellent wine and various liquors, honey and Mairēyaka [^561].  {#34:14}

The taste of white Lēśyā is infinitely better than that of dates, grapes, milk, candied and pounded sugar.  {#34:15}

The smell of the bad Lēśyās (viz. the three first) is infinitely worse than that of the corpse of a cow, dog, or snake.  {#34:16}

The smell of the three good Lēśyās is infinitely more pleasant than that of fragrant flowers and of perfumes when they are pounded.  {#34:17}

5\. The touch of the bad Lēśyās is infinitely worse than that of a saw, the tongue of a cow, or leaf of the Teak tree.  {#34:18}

The touch of the three good Lēśyās is infinitely more pleasant than that of cotton, butter, or Śirīsha-flowers [^562].  {#34:19}

6\. The degrees [^563] of the Lēśyās are three, or nine, [pg 199] or twenty-seven, or eighty-one, or two hundred and forty-three.  {#34:20}

7\. A man who acts on the impulse of the five Āsravas [^564], does not possess the three Guptis, has not ceased to injure the six (kinds of living beings), commits cruel acts, is wicked and violent, is afraid of no consequences [^565], is mischievous and does not subdue his senses--a man of such habits develops the black Lēśyā.  {#34:21}

A man of the following qualities: envy, anger, want of self-control, ignorance, deceit, want of modesty, greed, hatred, wickedness, carelessness, love of enjoyment; a man who pursues pleasures and does not abstain from sinful undertakings, who is wicked and violent--a man of such habits develops the blue Lēśyā.  {#34:23}
